Two Answers from Ahl al-Sunna on Perpetuity and Motion
Two responses to the perpetuity/motion dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two responses from Ahl al-Sunna regarding the timing of perpetuity and motion, and understand their different conceptions of motion.

Core Idea
Motion and perpetuity are explained either as occurring in the second moment or as two separate 'beings'.

Having presented the dilemma, the author now reports two solutions from Ahl al-Sunna. One group, including al-Ash'ari, equates motion and perpetuity, arguing that both occur in the second time. Another group, led by Ibn al-Rawandi and al-Qalanisi, differentiates by interpreting motion as two successive 'beings' (kawnān). Both answers defend the reality of perpetuity as an accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Read the passage to find the two groups: those who answered both questions with one answer (equating motion and perpetuity), and those who differentiated between them.
The first group includes Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Ash'ari; the second includes Ibn al-Rawandi and al-Qalanisi.
2
Understand the equating view
This group says motion occurs while the body is already in the second place; similarly, perpetuity occurs in the second time. The body/mover does not need to 'be' in a place it already is.
On this view, the substance is originated in the first moment, not remaining, and becomes remaining in the second by a new perpetuity.
3
Understand the differentiating view
This group argues that 'motion' is not a single accident but the name for two accidents: two 'beings' (kawnān), one in the first place and one in the second. Until the second being occurs, there is no motion.
This avoids the dilemma because the motion is not completed until the second being.
4
Note the authorities
Recognize the prominent scholars mentioned: Abu al-Hudhayl, al-Ash'ari, Ibn al-Rawandi, al-Qalanisi. Understand that this is a technical debate within Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر أسئلتهم التي يعولون عليها في إبطال قولنا، فمن ذلك قولهم: إن الباقي لو كان باقيًا ببقاء لم يخل البقاء من أن يكون حادثا في حال حدوثه أو في الثاني، فإن حدث في حال حدوث الجوهر وجب أن يكون باقيا فيه لوجود البقاء له، وإن كان حادثا في الثاني لم يبق به من الأول. والجواب عنه: أن هذا سؤال مأخوذ من سؤال الملحدين في نفي الأعراض، وهو قولهم: لو كان الجسم متحركًا بحركة لم تخل حركتها من أن تكون حادثة في المكان الأول أو تكون حادثة وهو في المكان الثاني، فإن حدثت الحركة والجسم في المكان الأول لكانت الحركة موجودة والجسم ليس بمتحرك ولم يجب خروجه عن مكانه لوجودها، وإن حدثت وهي في المكان الثاني استغن بكونه فيه عن حركة ينتقل بها إليه. ثم من أصحابنا من أجاب عن السؤالين جميعًا بجواب وحد وسوى بين الحركة والبقاء وقال: إن الحركة تحدث والجسم في المكان الثاني والبقاء يحدث في الوقت الثاني، وإنما كان كذلك لأن المتحرك إنما يكون متحركا بخروجه عن الأول وكونه في الثاني، واحتاج إلى حدوث الكون فيه لأن خروجه عن الأول نفى لكونه فيه ولا يحتاج إلى الكون في مكان ليس بكائن فيه. وهذا المذهب في الحركة مذهب أبي الهذيل وجماعة من المعتزلة، وإليه ذهب الأشعري. والباقي يكون باقيًا في الوقت الثاني واحتاج إلى البقاء فيه وليس ببقاء عن الأول إلى الثاني، وإنما يكون محدثًا في الأول غير باق ثم يكون باقيًا في الثاني بحدوث البقاء فيه. ومنهم من فرق بين الحركة والبقاء، وأجاب عن سؤال الحركة أن ليس باسم لعرض واحد بل هو اسم لعرضين وهما كونان أحدهما في المكان الأول، والآخر في المكان الثاني، فما لم يوجد الثاني لا يكون متحركًا فلا تكون الحركة موجودة في المكان الأول ولا في الثاني بل يتم في الثاني، وإليه ذهب ابن الراوندي في آخر عمره، وهو مذهب أبي العباس القلانسي وجماعة من المتكلمين: يتبين ذلك بعد هذا إن شاء الله تعالى.
Then we mention their questions that they rely on in invalidating our statement. Among them is their saying: If the remaining (al-bāqī) is remaining by a perpetuity (bi-baqā'), the perpetuity is not free from being either occurring (ḥādith) in the moment of its occurrence or in the second. If it occurs in the moment of the occurrence of the substance, then it must be remaining in it due to the existence of perpetuity for it. And if it occurs in the second, then it does not remain by it from the first. The answer to it: This question is taken from the question of the atheists (al-mulḥidīn) in negating accidents. It is their saying: If a body were moving by a motion, the motion is not free from being occurring in the first place or occurring while it is in the second place. If the motion occurs while the body is in the first place, then the motion would exist and the body would not be moving, and it would not be necessary for it to leave its place due to its existence. And if it occurs while it is in the second place, then it would be independent by its being in it from a motion by which it transfers to it. Then among our companions are those who answered both questions with one answer and equated between motion and perpetuity, saying: Motion occurs while the body is in the second place, and perpetuity occurs in the second time. It is like that because the mover is only moving by its leaving the first and being in the second, and it needs the occurrence of being (al-kawn) in it because its leaving the first negates its being in it, and it does not need being in a place in which it is not. This doctrine of motion is the doctrine of Abu al-Hudhayl and a group of the Mu'tazila, and al-Ash'ari adopted it. And the remaining (al-bāqī) is remaining in the second time and needs perpetuity in it, and it is not a perpetuity from the first to the second; rather, it is originated (muḥdath) in the first, not remaining, then it becomes remaining in the second by the occurrence of perpetuity in it. And among them are those who differentiated between motion and perpetuity, and answered the question of motion that it is not a name for a single accident but a name for two accidents, which are two 'beings' (kawnān), one in the first place and the other in the second place. So as long as the second has not come into existence, it is not moving, so the motion does not exist in the first place nor in the second; rather, it is completed in the second. This was adopted by Ibn al-Rawandi in the latter part of his life, and it is the doctrine of Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi and a group of theologians. This will become clear after this, God willing.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a key objection from the opponents (likely the Mu'tazila) against the Maturidi claim that an existing thing remains by a perpetuity (baqā') that is an accident. They ask: if a substance remains by a perpetuity, is that perpetuity happening at the first moment (when the substance is originated) or in the second moment? If it is in the first moment, then the substance must be remaining from the first moment. If it is in the second moment, then it does not remain by that perpetuity from the first moment. The author states that this question is based on a similar question used by atheists (al-mulḥidūn) to negate accidents. The atheists asked about motion: if a body moves by a motion, does the motion occur in the first place or the second? If in the first, the body would not actually be moving; if in the second, it wouldn't need motion to get there. The author then presents two answers from the Ahl al-Sunna: (1) Some scholars, including Abu al-Hudhayl (a Mu'tazili) and al-Ash'ari, answered both questions by equating motion and perpetuity. They say motion occurs while the body is already in the second place; similarly, perpetuity occurs in the second time. The being (kawn) in the second place is needed because leaving the first negates being there, but being in the second is not needed because without it there is no being. On this view, the substance is originated (muḥdath) in the first moment, not remaining, then it becomes remaining in the second by the occurrence of perpetuity. (2) Other scholars, such as Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, differentiated between motion and perpetuity. They argue that 'motion' is not a single accident but the name for two accidents: a being (kawn) in the first place and a being in the second place. Until the second being occurs, the body is not moving. Thus motion is not present in the first or second but is completed in the second. The author notes this will be clarified later.

Key Terms
باقي (bāqī)
بقاء (baqā')
حركة (ḥaraka)
كون (kawn)
أبو الهذيل (Abu al-Hudhayl)
الأشعري (al-Ash'arī)
ابن الراوندي (Ibn al-Rawandi)
أبو العباس القلانسي (Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all Ahl al-Sunna subscribe to the same explanation of motion and perpetuity.
Correction: The passage shows there were two distinct views: one equating motion and perpetuity (held by al-Ash'ari among others) and one differentiating (held by Ibn al-Rawandi and al-Qalanisi).

Checkpoint
According to the first answer in the passage, when does perpetuity (al-baqā') occur?
A) In the first moment.
B) In the second time.
C) Simultaneously with the essence.
D) It is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna offered two defenses of perpetuity as an accident: one by equating it with motion's timing, and another by reinterpreting motion as two successive beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is renewed by an accident of perpetuity at every moment, how does that change the way you rely on Allah for the next breath? What would it mean to live each instant conscious of that dependence?
?
The theologians debated whether motion is a single accident or two. But you experience movement constantly. In what areas of your life are you moving without being mindful of the One who creates your every 'being' in each place? How can you turn that moving into worship?
?
This passage shows two ways to preserve God's eternity while explaining change. But the real question for you is: do you live as if God's perpetuity is a distant concept, or as if He holds you in existence every moment? What practical change would it take to live the latter today?

The Mu'tazila Accused of Hypocrisy and True Jabr
Exposes Mu'tazila inconsistency and notes scholars who called them Jabriyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's internal contradiction regarding human power and justice, and why some Sunni scholars labeled them 'Jabriyya'.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila, who oppose Jabr, actually endorse a worse form of compulsion.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi exposes a remarkable irony in Mu'tazila theology. They pride themselves on defending human free will (ikhtiyār) and divine justice (ʿadl), yet their own doctrines strip the agent of real power and imply that God punishes for the impossible. Consequently, some Sunni scholars turned their own label against them, calling the Mu'tazila 'Jabriyya'—the very name they used for their opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the tone: the author expresses amazement at Mu'tazila audacity.
This is a polemical passage, not a neutral description.
2
Identify the two contradictory positions
The passage contrasts two pairs: how Mu'tazila label others vs. how they label themselves. Find each pair in the text.
Look for 'ينسبون إلى الجبر' and 'يسمون أنفسهم'.
3
Analyze the accusation of impudence
The author concludes that the Mu'tazila either lack shame or are profoundly ignorant. Why is this conclusion drawn? Reflect on the logical inconsistency.
The inconsistency lies in claiming justice while denying power.
4
Notice the Sunni response
The passage ends by stating that some Sunni verifiers called the Mu'tazila 'Jabriyya'. What does this label imply?
This is a rhetorical reversal.
5
Consider the broader kalām significance
This critique is not just name-calling; it targets a core contradiction in Mu'tazila theology. How does it relate to the topic of qadar (predestination)?
This passage prepares for a deeper discussion on human power and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العجب من وقاحة المعتزلة وقلة حيائهم حيث ينسبون إلى الجبر من يقول: إن المكلف يفعل عن قدرة لا عن عجز، ويسمون أنفسهم القائلين بالاختيار مع زعمهم أن لا قدرة للفاعل على فعله ويسمون أنفسهم أهل العدل مع زعمهم أن الله تعالى يعذب العباد على أفعال لا قدرة لهم عليها، ويكلفهم بأفعال لا تعلق لها باستطاعة المكلف بوجه من الوجوه، ويسمون القائلين بأنه تعالى لا يعذب أحدًا على فعل لا قدرة له عليه ولا يأمره بذلك ولا ينهاه عنه أهل التجوير. وهذا غاية الوقاحة أو نهاية الجهل والغباوة.
ولهذا الحرف كان بعض المحققين (۱) من أهل السنة يسمى المعتزلة مجبرة وكثيرا (۲) ما كان (۳) يقول : قالت المعتزلة المجبرة كذا،
Then amazement at the impudence of the Mu'tazila and their lack of shame, where they attribute to Jabr the one who says: the responsible agent acts by power, not by incapacity, and they call themselves the proponents of free will while claiming that the agent has no power over his act, and they call themselves the people of justice while claiming that Allah Almighty punishes servants for acts over which they have no power, and charges them with acts that have no connection to the servant's capacity in any way, and they call those who say that He Almighty does not punish anyone for an act over which he has no power, nor command him to it nor forbid him from it, the people of tajwīr (declaring injustice). And this is the ultimate impudence or the utmost ignorance and stupidity. And for this reason, some of the verifiers among Ahl al-Sunna used to call the Mu'tazila 'Jabriyya' and often he would say: 'The Mu'tazila Jabriyya said such-and-such.'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a sharp rhetorical condemnation of the Mu'tazila. It begins by expressing astonishment at their audacity: they accuse others of Jabr (compulsion) for affirming that the responsible agent acts by power, not by incapacity. Meanwhile, they call themselves proponents of free will (ikhtiyār) despite asserting that the agent has no power at the moment of action. They also call themselves 'the people of justice' (ahl al-ʿadl) while claiming that Allah punishes people for acts they cannot perform and imposes obligations beyond their capacity. In contrast, they label those who deny that Allah would punish for the impossible as 'people of tajwīr' (ascribing injustice to God). The author declares this the height of impudence and ignorance. The second part of the passage notes that some verifying Sunni scholars consequently called the Mu'tazila 'Jabriyya', often prefacing their citations with 'the Mu'tazila Jabriyya said'. This highlights the ironic inversion: the Mu'tazila, who pride themselves on opposing Jabr, are in fact the true exponents of compulsion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila are consistent defenders of free will and justice.
Correction: As Nasafi shows, their denial of real human power and their claim that God punishes for impossible acts contradict their own principles, leading Sunni scholars to label them as the true Jabriyya.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila, despite claiming free will and justice, logically entail a form of compulsion (Jabr) for which Sunni scholars dubbed them 'Jabriyya'.

The Mu'tazilite Contradiction: Accusing Others of Jabr While Holding It
Nasafi rebukes Mu'tazilites for hypocrisy, labeling them mujbira.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some Sunni scholars label the Mu'tazilites as 'mujbira' and how this critique exposes an internal inconsistency in their doctrine of free will.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites' own denial of power entails compulsion, not free will.

In this passage, Imām al-Nasafī presents a sharp critique of the Mu'tazilites, pointing out a fundamental contradiction in their theology. They claim to uphold free will (ikhtiyār) and justice (‘adl), yet their own position—denying the agent any power at the moment of action—actually implies coercion (jabr). Nasafī highlights this irony and notes that some Sunni scholars therefore refer to the Mu'tazilites as 'mujbira' (those who hold to compulsion). This lesson explores that critique and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then follow with the literal translation to grasp the flow of Nasafī's argument.
Notice the tone: Nasafī expresses astonishment at their 'insolence' and 'lack of shame'.
2
Identify the three contradictions
Nasafī lists three specific inconsistencies: (1) they call others who affirm power 'jabari', (2) they call themselves 'ahl al-ikhtiyār' while denying power, (3) they call themselves 'ahl al-‘adl' while claiming God punishes for impossible acts. Find each in the text.
3
Understand the label 'mujbira'
Reflect on why some Sunni scholars call the Mu'tazilites 'mujbira': because their doctrine, despite their claims, actually leads to compulsion. Consider how this label is not mere insult but a doctrinal assessment.
Nasafī quotes a scholar who used to say 'the Mu'tazilite mujbira said...'

Then one wonders at the insolence of the Mu'tazilites and their lack of shame, for they attribute to coercion (jabr) the one who says that the obligated person acts by power not by incapacity, and they call themselves the advocates of free will (ikhtiyar) while claiming that the agent has no power over his action, and they call themselves the people of justice ('adl) while claiming that God Almighty punishes servants for actions over which they have no power, and obligates them to actions that have no connection whatsoever to the capability of the obligated person, and they call those who say that He Almighty does not punish anyone for an action over which he has no power, nor does He command him or forbid him, the people of injustice (tajwir). This is the height of insolence or the ultimate ignorance and stupidity. And for this reason, some investigators (muhaqqiqun) from Ahl al-Sunnah used to call the Mu'tazilites 'mujbira' (compellers), and he would often say: 'The Mu'tazilite mujbira said such and such.'
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a rhetorical critique, not a formal proof. The author highlights the inconsistency of the Mu'tazilites: they accuse those who affirm power at the time of action of being predestinarians (jabriyya), yet they themselves deny the agent any power at the moment of action, which is the actual position of compulsion. They claim to uphold justice but believe God punishes for acts one cannot perform. The author then notes that some Sunni scholars therefore call the Mu'tazilites 'mujbira' (those who hold to coercion) because their doctrine leads to compulsion, not free will.

The Mu'tazila's First Objection on Motion and Rest
The Mu'tazila's first objection on motion/rest and its refutation via definitions of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first objection regarding motion and rest and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between different definitions of motion and rest.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's objection fails because it assumes a definition of motion that not all accept.

The Mu'tazila argued that a body must initially be devoid of motion and rest, but later cannot be. This seems to challenge the idea that a body always has an accident. The author responds by pointing out that this objection only holds under a specific definition of motion; under another definition, the body initially has an accident of the same genus, so it is not actually devoid. This lesson examines that objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's first objection
Read the passage that states the first objection: that a body may be free of motion and rest initially, then cannot later.
2
Identify the two definitions of motion
Notice that the author discusses two definitions: motion as a single kawn in the second time with a condition, and motion as two kawn in two places.
These definitions are key to the refutation.
3
Understand why the objection does not apply under the first definition
Under the first definition, the body initially has a kawn of the same genus as motion, so it is not devoid.
4
See how the objection appears plausible under the second definition but fails
Under the second definition, the body would need two kawn initially, but that is impossible; however, the objection also fails because the body never lacks a single kawn at any time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض للمعتزلة]

فاعترضوا على هذا الكلام بفصلين كل واحد منهما للتفرقة بين وقت واحد وبين أوقات كثيرة.

أحدهما: أن الجسم يجوز خلوه عن الحركة والسكون بل يجب ذلك في أول أحوال وجوده ثم يستحيل ذلك في الوقت الثاني والثالث.

والآخر أن الصحيح والسليم غير قادر على الفعل أول كونه ثم لم يجز أبدًا.

[الرد عليهم]

والجواب: أن السؤال الأول يتوجه على بعض الخصوم دون البعض، ومن توجه عليه من حيث الظاهر فهو إما مغالطة منهم مع علمهم أنه غير لازم، وإما جهل بالحقائق.

وبيان هذا أن من قال الحركة اسم لكون واحد وهو الكون الموجود في الزمان الثاني إلا أن شرط استحقاقه هذا الاسم بعدم كون في مكان آخر، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون، وهو من جنس ما يسمى حركة إلا أنه لا يسمى به لفقد شرط وهو بعدم كون له في مكان آخر، فإذا لم يخل الجسم في أول أحوال الوجود عما هو من جنس الحركة فلم يكن هذا الاعتراض متوجها عليهم. وإنما يتوجه من حيث الظاهر على من يقول : إن الحركة اسم لكونين في مكانين والسكون اسم لكونين في مكان واحد، وشرط الكونين زمانان لأنهما فعلان فلا يوجدان إلا في زمانين، إذ في كل زمان يوجد فيه فصل واحد، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون وهو فعل واحد، وهم ألزموا خلو القدرة عن الفعل أصلاً في زمان واحد فعارضوا بخلو الجسم عن فعلين في زمان واحد، وخلو الجسم عن كونين جائز في كل وقت من أوقات البقاء بل واجب كما هو جائز بل واجب في أول أحوال وجوده. فإذا قط لا يوجد في زمان ما للجسم إلا كون واحد، ولا يخلو عنه في زمان ما لا زمان وجوده ولا زمان بقائه.

فأما القدرة فإنها خالية عن الفعل حال وجودها وإن تحقق ذاتها، ويستحيل بعد ذلك خلوها عن الفعل وإن لم تتغير ذاتها، فالمعترض بفعل خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون إن كان عالما بما بينا من حقيقة الحركة والسكون واستحالة خلو الجسم في أول أوقاته عن كون، واستحالة وجود كونين في حال ما وإن كانت حالة البقاء وثبوت المساواة بين الأحوال أجمع في وجوب وجود كون فيه واستحالة وجود كونين فيه مع علمه بذلك اعترض بهذا الاعتراض؛ فهو مغالط، وإن لم يكن عالمًا بذلك كان السؤال صادرا عن الجهل بالحقائق.
Objection of the Mu'tazila. They objected to this statement with two points, each one for differentiating between a single time and many times. One of them: that the body may be free of motion and rest, indeed that is necessary in the first state of its existence, then that becomes impossible in the second and third time. The other: that the healthy and sound person is not capable of action at the first moment of his existence, then it is never permissible. The response: The first question applies to some opponents not others, and whoever it applies to apparently, it is either sophistry on their part knowing it is not necessary, or ignorance of realities. The explanation of this: that he who says motion is the name of a single accident (kawn) which is the accident existing in the second time, except that the condition for its deserving this name is the absence of an accident in another place, and the body in the first state of its existence is not devoid of an accident, and it is of the same genus as what is called motion except that it is not called by it due to the loss of a condition, which is the absence of an accident for it in another place. So when the body was not devoid in the first states of existence of what is of the genus of motion, then this objection was not applicable to them. And it applies apparently only to the one who says: motion is the name of two accidents in two places, and rest is the name of two accidents in one place, and the condition of the two accidents is two times because they are two acts and they only exist in two times, since in each time only a single particle (fasl) exists, and the body in the first state of its existence is not devoid of an accident which is a single act, and they necessitated the voiding of power from act entirely in a single time, so they objected by the voiding of the body of two acts in a single time, and the voiding of the body of two accidents is permissible at every time of the times of continuance, indeed obligatory, just as it is permissible, indeed obligatory, in the first state of its existence. So indeed the body never exists at any time without a single accident, and it is not devoid of it at any time, neither the time of its existence nor the time of its continuance. As for power, it is devoid of act at the moment of its existence even if its essence is realized, and it is impossible after that for it to be devoid of act even if its essence does not change. So the objector concerning the voiding of the body of motion and rest, if he knows what we have explained about the reality of motion and rest and the impossibility of the voiding of the body in its first times of an accident, and the impossibility of the existence of two accidents in any state even if it is a state of continuance, and the confirmation of equality among all states in the necessity of the existence of an accident in it and the impossibility of the existence of two accidents in it, and with his knowledge of that he objects with this objection, then he is a sophist. And if he is not knowledgeable of that, then the question arises from ignorance of realities.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage records an objection from the Mu'tazila and provides the author's refutation. The objection has two parts. First, the Mu'tazila argue that a body must initially be devoid of motion and rest (since it cannot have both at once), but later it can have them. Second, they argue that a person initially lacks the power to act, and never gains it (implying that power must exist before the action). The author responds by first noting that the first objection only applies to those who define motion and rest in a particular way. Specifically, for those who define motion as a single accident (kawn) occurring in the second time conditionally upon absence of another kawn elsewhere, the body initially has a kawn of the same genus as motion, so it is not actually devoid. The objection only holds against those who define motion as two kawn in two places and rest as two kawn in one place, because that would require two acts at once initially, which is impossible. The author then addresses the second objection regarding power, stating that power can initially be devoid of act without contradiction, but later it cannot be devoid. Thus the objector is either a sophist or ignorant of the realities.

Key Terms
كون
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila's objection is universally valid, but it only applies to a specific definition of motion and rest.
Correction: Under the definition of motion as a single kawn with condition, the body initially has a kawn of that genus, so it is not devoid. Under the definition as two kawn, the body can be without two kawn.

Checkpoint
According to the author, under which definition of motion does the Mu'tazila's first objection appear to apply?
A) Motion as a single kawn in the second time with a condition
B) Motion as two kawn in two places
C) Motion as a single kawn in the first time
D) Motion as the absence of rest

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first objection is not universally valid; it only holds under a particular definition of motion that not all scholars accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed an opposing argument simply because it assumed a definition you don't accept? When you find yourself rejecting someone's conclusion, do you first check whether your disagreement is actually about the premises or the definitions? Go to that memory now and ask yourself: 'What definition was I assuming, and was it the only valid one?' Commit to identifying one unexamined assumption in a current disagreement this week.
?
The Mu'tazila built a whole objection on a specific definition of motion without considering that others might define it differently. Is there an area of your belief or practice where you hold onto a position without realizing it depends on an unstated definition? Write down one theological or practical conviction you hold, and then explicitly write the definition of its key term as you understand it. Then ask: 'Is this definition universally accepted by others who disagree with me?' If not, your conviction may rest on a narrower foundation than you thought.
?
The author does not reject the Mu'tazila's entire system; he simply shows their objection fails under a different definition. This is a model of precise, charitable critique. Think of a person (or group) whose views you find wrong. Instead of attacking them broadly, what is the single key concept (like 'motion' in this passage) where a different definition could change the entire argument? Challenge yourself to learn how they define that concept from their own sources, not from your assumptions. Then ask: 'Am I still convinced they are wrong?'

The Mu'tazila's Second Objection on Power and Act
The Mu'tazila's second objection on power/act and its refutation via the nature of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's second objection regarding power and action and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between the state of power before and after activation.

Core Idea
Power can initially be devoid of act without contradiction, but later it cannot; the objection confuses the two states.

The Mu'tazila also argued that a person initially lacks the power to act, and never gains it, implying that power must precede the act. The author responds by clarifying that power is initially devoid of act but then necessarily acts, so the objection is not valid. This lesson examines that second objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the passage that states the second objection: that a healthy person is not capable of action at the first moment of existence, then it is never permissible.
2
Understand the author's distinction about power
Notice that the author says power is devoid of act at its existence, but after that it cannot be devoid.
This is key to the refutation.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the objector is either a sophist (if aware of the reality) or ignorant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض للمعتزلة]

فاعترضوا على هذا الكلام بفصلين كل واحد منهما للتفرقة بين وقت واحد وبين أوقات كثيرة.

أحدهما: أن الجسم يجوز خلوه عن الحركة والسكون بل يجب ذلك في أول أحوال وجوده ثم يستحيل ذلك في الوقت الثاني والثالث.

والآخر أن الصحيح والسليم غير قادر على الفعل أول كونه ثم لم يجز أبدًا.

[الرد عليهم]

والجواب: أن السؤال الأول يتوجه على بعض الخصوم دون البعض، ومن توجه عليه من حيث الظاهر فهو إما مغالطة منهم مع علمهم أنه غير لازم، وإما جهل بالحقائق.

وبيان هذا أن من قال الحركة اسم لكون واحد وهو الكون الموجود في الزمان الثاني إلا أن شرط استحقاقه هذا الاسم بعدم كون في مكان آخر، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون، وهو من جنس ما يسمى حركة إلا أنه لا يسمى به لفقد شرط وهو بعدم كون له في مكان آخر، فإذا لم يخل الجسم في أول أحوال الوجود عما هو من جنس الحركة فلم يكن هذا الاعتراض متوجها عليهم. وإنما يتوجه من حيث الظاهر على من يقول : إن الحركة اسم لكونين في مكانين والسكون اسم لكونين في مكان واحد، وشرط الكونين زمانان لأنهما فعلان فلا يوجدان إلا في زمانين، إذ في كل زمان يوجد فيه فصل واحد، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون وهو فعل واحد، وهم ألزموا خلو القدرة عن الفعل أصلاً في زمان واحد فعارضوا بخلو الجسم عن فعلين في زمان واحد، وخلو الجسم عن كونين جائز في كل وقت من أوقات البقاء بل واجب كما هو جائز بل واجب في أول أحوال وجوده. فإذا قط لا يوجد في زمان ما للجسم إلا كون واحد، ولا يخلو عنه في زمان ما لا زمان وجوده ولا زمان بقائه.

فأما القدرة فإنها خالية عن الفعل حال وجودها وإن تحقق ذاتها، ويستحيل بعد ذلك خلوها عن الفعل وإن لم تتغير ذاتها، فالمعترض بفعل خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون إن كان عالما بما بينا من حقيقة الحركة والسكون واستحالة خلو الجسم في أول أوقاته عن كون، واستحالة وجود كونين في حال ما وإن كانت حالة البقاء وثبوت المساواة بين الأحوال أجمع في وجوب وجود كون فيه واستحالة وجود كونين فيه مع علمه بذلك اعترض بهذا الاعتراض؛ فهو مغالط، وإن لم يكن عالمًا بذلك كان السؤال صادرا عن الجهل بالحقائق.
Objection of the Mu'tazila. They objected to this statement with two points, each one for differentiating between a single time and many times. One of them: that the body may be free of motion and rest, indeed that is necessary in the first state of its existence, then that becomes impossible in the second and third time. The other: that the healthy and sound person is not capable of action at the first moment of his existence, then it is never permissible. The response: The first question applies to some opponents not others, and whoever it applies to apparently, it is either sophistry on their part knowing it is not necessary, or ignorance of realities. The explanation of this: that he who says motion is the name of a single accident (kawn) which is the accident existing in the second time, except that the condition for its deserving this name is the absence of an accident in another place, and the body in the first state of its existence is not devoid of an accident, and it is of the same genus as what is called motion except that it is not called by it due to the loss of a condition, which is the absence of an accident for it in another place. So when the body was not devoid in the first states of existence of what is of the genus of motion, then this objection was not applicable to them. And it applies apparently only to the one who says: motion is the name of two accidents in two places, and rest is the name of two accidents in one place, and the condition of the two accidents is two times because they are two acts and they only exist in two times, since in each time only a single particle (fasl) exists, and the body in the first state of its existence is not devoid of an accident which is a single act, and they necessitated the voiding of power from act entirely in a single time, so they objected by the voiding of the body of two acts in a single time, and the voiding of the body of two accidents is permissible at every time of the times of continuance, indeed obligatory, just as it is permissible, indeed obligatory, in the first state of its existence. So indeed the body never exists at any time without a single accident, and it is not devoid of it at any time, neither the time of its existence nor the time of its continuance. As for power, it is devoid of act at the moment of its existence even if its essence is realized, and it is impossible after that for it to be devoid of act even if its essence does not change. So the objector concerning the voiding of the body of motion and rest, if he knows what we have explained about the reality of motion and rest and the impossibility of the voiding of the body in its first times of an accident, and the impossibility of the existence of two accidents in any state even if it is a state of continuance, and the confirmation of equality among all states in the necessity of the existence of an accident in it and the impossibility of the existence of two accidents in it, and with his knowledge of that he objects with this objection, then he is a sophist. And if he is not knowledgeable of that, then the question arises from ignorance of realities.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage records an objection from the Mu'tazila and provides the author's refutation. The objection has two parts. First, the Mu'tazila argue that a body must initially be devoid of motion and rest (since it cannot have both at once), but later it can have them. Second, they argue that a person initially lacks the power to act, and never gains it (implying that power must exist before the action). The author responds by first noting that the first objection only applies to those who define motion and rest in a particular way. Specifically, for those who define motion as a single accident (kawn) occurring in the second time conditionally upon absence of another kawn elsewhere, the body initially has a kawn of the same genus as motion, so it is not actually devoid. The objection only holds against those who define motion as two kawn in two places and rest as two kawn in one place, because that would require two acts at once initially, which is impossible. The author then addresses the second objection regarding power, stating that power can initially be devoid of act without contradiction, but later it cannot be devoid. Thus the objector is either a sophist or ignorant of the realities.

Key Terms
قدرة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think power must always be accompanied by act, but the author shows it can be initially devoid.
Correction: Power is created to produce an act; at its first moment it is devoid, but it necessarily produces the act afterward.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the state of power at the moment of its existence?
A) It is always accompanied by an act.
B) It is devoid of act even if its essence is realized.
C) It necessarily has an act.
D) It cannot exist without an act.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's second objection fails because power is initially devoid of act but necessarily produces act afterward; no contradiction exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel powerless to begin a good deed, do you believe that Allah will create the ability the moment you resolve to act? How might this lesson free you from procrastination?
?
Have you ever misjudged your own capability because you expected to feel fully ready before starting? What does the distinction between the initial and subsequent state of power teach you about taking the first step?
?
The Mu'tazila stumbled by demanding that power must always be with act. In your life, where have you demanded certainty before acting, missing the grace that comes only when you move? Will you now act on faith before you see the power?

Shaykh Abu Mansur's Refutation on Motion and Rest
Shaykh Abu Mansur gives an alternative refutation focusing on the impossibility of two accidents in the first moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Shaykh Abu Mansur's refutation of the Mu'tazila objection regarding motion and rest, focusing on the impossibility of two accidents at the first moment and the distinction between initial and subsequent states.

Core Idea
The first moment of existence is unique; two accidents cannot coexist there, but later they can.

Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi provides a precise theological refutation to a Mu'tazila argument about motion and rest. He identifies a key ontological distinction between the first moment of existence and later continuity. This passage establishes the foundational principle that what is impossible initially may become possible later, and warns against faulty analogies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the Shaykh's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the statement that a body cannot be moving or resting in the first moment of existence. Note the reason given: the impossibility (ihala) because each motion/rest is a name for two accidents (kawin) that cannot exist simultaneously at that moment.
The term 'kawin' is technical; it means two accidents like motion and rest or their opposites.
3
Understand the distinction
Notice the contrast between the first moment and the state of continuance (baqa'). In continuance, there is no impossibility, so the distinction (farq) occurs. This is the key point that undermines the Mu'tazila analogy.
The Shaykh uses this distinction to show that the initial impossibility does not apply later.
4
Examine the rejection of analogy
Read how the Shaykh criticizes the Mu'tazila's qiyas: analogizing something without impossibility to something with impossibility is an invalid analogy without a common cause (illa). He demands that if they claim impossibility, they must prove it directly.
This is a methodological point about proper reasoning in kalam.
5
Connect to the broader debate
The passage ends by applying this principle to the question of when a person becomes capable of motion. Understand that the same logic applies: the first moment is unique, and later moments allow for capacity.
This shows how the Shaykh's answer resolves multiple objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب الشيخ أبو منصور - رحمه الله - وقال : إنما لم يجز أن يكون متحركًا أو ساكنا في أول أحوال وجوده لما فيه من الإحالة، فإن كل واحد منهما اسم كونين، وجودهما في أول وجوده محال، ولا إحالة في حالة البقاء فوقع الفرق. فأما فيما نحن فيه فلا استحالة، وقياس ما لا استحالة في ثبوته على ما لثبوته استحالة في المنع قياس من غير علة جامعة.

وإن قال فيما نحن فيه استحالة، فالواجب عليه بيان الاستحالة دون الاشتغال بإيراد النظر.

وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن الإنسان متى يكون قادرًا على الحركة، يقدر
And Shaykh Abu Mansur - may God have mercy on him - answered and said: It is not permissible for it to be moving or resting in the first state of its existence because of the impossibility in that, for each of them is the name of two accidents, and their existence in its first existence is impossible, and there is no impossibility in the state of continuance, so the distinction occurs. As for what we are in, there is no impossibility, and analogizing that which has no impossibility in its affirmation to that whose affirmation involves impossibility is an analogy without a common cause. And if he says that there is impossibility in what we are in, then he must demonstrate the impossibility without busying himself with presenting the argument. And with this they are answered regarding their statement: 'When is a person capable of motion? He is capable...'
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the response of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi to the same Mu'tazila objection. He argues that motion and rest are each names for two accidents (kawin). In the first moment of a body's existence, two accidents cannot exist simultaneously, so the body cannot be moving or resting. After the first moment (in the state of continuance), there is no such impossibility, so motion and rest become possible. The Shaykh then rejects the analogy drawn by the Mu'tazila between the initial impossibility and later possibility, stating that such an analogy lacks a common cause (illa). He further insists that if the objector claims impossibility in the present case (i.e., that human power is absent initially), he must provide proof of that impossibility rather than merely presenting an argument without demonstration. Finally, the passage indicates that this response also answers the Mu'tazila's question about when a person becomes capable of motion, implying that the same principle applies: the first moment is unique, and later moments allow for capacity.

Key Terms
إحالة
كونين
قياس
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shaykh's argument is arbitrary and relies on an unexplained exception for the first moment.
Correction: The argument is based on a clear ontological principle: two accidents cannot exist simultaneously in the first moment of a body's existence. This is not arbitrary but follows from the nature of accidents and the uniqueness of the initial instant.

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental reason Shaykh Abu Mansur gives for the body not being moving or resting in the first moment?

B) Because the body does not yet exist in the first moment.
C) Because motion and rest are defined as the absence of each other.
D) Because the first moment is a time of pure potentiality.

Key Takeaway
Shaykh Abu Mansur argues that the impossibility of motion and rest in the first moment stems from the impossibility of two accidents coexisting at that instant, and that this does not apply to later states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Does this humbling truth remind you that your very ability to act is given to you later by Allah, not from yourself? How does this change your view of your own efforts?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to analogize a situation where impossibility exists (first moment) to one where it does not (later). Are there areas in your life where you have falsely extended a past limitation into the present, assuming your current situation is beyond hope? What would it mean to accept that Allah can grant capacities after an initial state of incapability?
?
Shaykh Abu Mansur demands that if the opponent claims impossibility, they must provide direct demonstration. In your spiritual journey, are there claims of impossibility (e.g., 'I can never give up this sin') that you accept without proof? How would your life change if you demanded evidence for those claims?

Maturidi's Answer on Motion and Rest at First Creation
Maturidi explains why motion or rest is impossible at the first moment of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argued that motion and rest are impossible at the first moment of existence and how he refutes a false analogy.

Core Idea
Motion and rest each name two states, making them impossible at the first instant of existence.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi addresses a theological argument about the attributes of capability and action. He explains why a body cannot be described as moving or at rest at the very first moment of its existence. He also refutes an attempted analogy by showing it lacks a common cause. This lesson breaks down his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a concise argumentative style.
2
Understand the key argument
Identify why motion and rest are impossible at the first moment. Maturidi says each term is a name for 'two states' (kawnayn). Consider what these two states are and why they cannot coexist at the initial instant.
Motion requires a previous state; rest requires a prior moment of rest.
3
Analyze the refutation of the analogy
Maturidi addresses an objection that tries to compare this case to another. He points out that the analogy lacks a common cause (illa jamia). Ask yourself: what is the difference between the two cases that makes the analogy invalid?
The objector must prove impossibility in the other case, not just assert it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب الشيخ أبو منصور - رحمه الله - وقال : إنما لم يجز أن يكون متحركًا أو ساكنا في أول أحوال وجوده لما فيه من الإحالة، فإن كل واحد منهما اسم كونين، وجودهما في أول وجوده محال، ولا إحالة في حالة البقاء فوقع الفرق. فأما فيما نحن فيه فلا استحالة، وقياس ما لا استحالة في ثبوته على ما لثبوته استحالة في المنع قياس من غير علة جامعة. وإن قال فيما نحن فيه استحالة، فالواجب عليه بيان الاستحالة دون الاشتغال بإيراد النظر.
And Sheikh Abu Mansur—may Allah have mercy on him—answered and said: The reason that it is not permissible for it to be moving or at rest in the first of its states of existence is due to the impossibility contained therein, for each one of them is a name for two states (kawnayn); their existence at the first of its existence is impossible, and there is no impossibility in the state of subsistence, so the difference occurs. As for what we are discussing, there is no impossibility, and analogizing that whose establishment has no impossibility upon that whose establishment has an impossibility in prohibition is an analogy without a common cause. And if he says that there is impossibility in what we are discussing, then it is incumbent upon him to explain the impossibility without busying himself with presenting the argument.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage records a response from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He explains why a body cannot be described as moving or at rest at the very first moment of its existence: because each term—motion and rest—is a name for two states. For example, motion requires the body to be in one place at one time and another place at the next, so at the first moment there is no prior state, making it impossible. However, during subsistence (baqa'), this is not a problem because the body already exists with a state. Maturidi then addresses an attempted analogy: someone might try to compare the case under discussion (capability and action) to the case of motion in the first moment. But the analogy fails because in the case of capability there is no impossibility, whereas in the first-moment case there is an impossibility. Therefore, the analogy lacks a common cause. If the objector insists that there is an impossibility in the capability case, he must prove it rather than just making objections.

Key Terms
كون
حركة
سكون
إحالة
بقاء
قياس
علة جامعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion and rest are simple attributes that can be applied at any moment, including the first moment of existence.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that each term is a name for two states (kawnayn), requiring a prior and subsequent condition, making them impossible at the first instant.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say motion or rest is impossible at the first moment?
A) Because the body does not yet have a location.
B) Because each term implies two states that cannot be simultaneous at the first moment.
C) Because the body is created without attributes.
D) Because motion requires time to pass.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's key point is that motion and rest are not simple attributes but names for two states, making them impossible at the first moment of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the first moment of your own existence (your birth, or your conversion), what attributes did you possess at that instant? Are you tempted to claim for yourself a perfection that only belongs to Allah, by assuming you were always as you are now? Must you not humbly admit that your very being is a gift that unfolded over time, and that even your righteous actions are not self-originating?
?
If Maturidi’s opponent had simply insisted on his analogy without proving the impossibility he claimed, he would have been refuted. In your own debates about religion, do you first examine your own assumptions for hidden impossibilities before making objections? Will you commit to demanding proof from yourself before challenging others?
?
The precision of this argument shows that Allah’s creation is governed by order and logic. Does your heart truly submit to the fact that Allah’s wisdom encompasses even the most subtle distinctions? Are you willing to let go of any belief that you hold based on habit rather than sound reasoning, even if it feels comfortable?

Refutation of the Objection on Initial Lack of Capability
Nasafi refutes the claim that a healthy person lacks capability at the start of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the claim that a healthy person initially lacks capability by affirming the necessary coexistence of capability with sound limbs.

Core Idea
Capability necessarily accompanies sound limbs from the very beginning.

This short passage addresses a theological objection about human capability at the moment of creation. Nasafi defends the Maturidi position that soundness of limbs and capability are inseparable, drawing on al-Ash'ari's own argument against the Mu'tazili al-Ka'bi. As you read, notice how the refutation hinges on a definitional principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first sentence to understand what the opponent claims: that a sound, healthy person initially lacks capability for action.
The objection is attributed to an unnamed source (likely Mu'tazili).
2
Understand the Maturidi principle
Nasafi cites 'our companions' who assert that a person is not considered sound unless capable. This is the key principle that makes the objection impossible.
This principle ties capability directly to physical soundness.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
Since capability necessarily exists whenever sound limbs exist, the objection's premise is false. The question is repelled.
Nasafi notes that al-Ash'ari used this same argument against al-Ka'bi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زعم أن الصحيح السليم يخلو عن القدرة على الفعل في أول خلقه ثم لا يجوز بعد ذلك. قلنا: من زعم من أصحابنا أن الإنسان لا يكون صحيح الجوارح سليمًا إلا إذا كان قادراً، كان هذا السؤال على أصله محالاً، لأنه لما وجدت صحة الجوارح وجدت القدرة ضرورة في أي حالة كانت، فاندفع هذا السؤال على أصله، اعتمد الأشعري في كتابه الذي رد فيه كتاب أوائل الأدلة لأبي القاسم الكعبي.
And what he claimed that the sound, healthy person is free of capability for action at the beginning of his creation, then it is not permissible after that. We said: Whoever among our companions claims that a person is not sound of limbs and healthy unless he is capable, this question according to his principle is impossible, because when the health of the limbs exists, capability exists necessarily in whatever state it is. So this question is repelled according to his principle. Al-Ash'ari relied on this in his book in which he refuted the book 'Awa'il al-Adilla' of Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection: a sound and healthy person is claimed to be without capability for action at the moment of his first creation, and then later capability becomes possible. The author responds by citing the view of his school (our companions): that a person is not considered sound of limbs and healthy unless he is capable. Therefore, on this principle, the question is impossible because health and capability are simultaneous. When health exists, capability necessarily exists. Thus the objection is invalid from the start. The author then notes that al-Ash'ari relied on this argument in his refutation of al-Ka'bi's book 'Awa'il al-Adilla.

Key Terms

سليم

جوارح

Misconception: Some think that capability is not present at the very beginning of a person's existence and appears later.
Correction: Nasafi argues that if a person is sound and healthy, capability necessarily exists from the start, because soundness implies capability.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between health of limbs and capability?
A) Health comes after capability.
B) Capability comes after health.
C) When health exists, capability necessarily exists.
D) They are independent of each other.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the claim of initial lack of capability by establishing that soundness of limbs necessarily entails the presence of capability from the very beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim you want to obey Allah but find yourself incapable. Look at your limbs: they are sound. What prevents you from using the capability Allah has already given you? Are you using your health to draw closer to Him or to distance yourself?
?
If capability necessarily accompanies soundness, then every healthy moment is an obligation to act. Where in your daily life have you excused yourself from good deeds due to 'lack of ability' while your limbs were fully capable? What will you change today?
?
The objection in this lesson is dismissed as impossible because soundness implies capability. But in your own life, you may have believed you lacked capability despite being sound. Is that belief a form of self-deception? How will you confront the excuses you have made to avoid action?

The Mu'tazilite Middle State Argument
Mu'tazilite argument for a middle state between capacity and incapacity during the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument for a middle state between capacity and incapacity during the act, including the reasoning that the agent is neither capable nor incapable.

Core Idea
The agent during the act is neither capable nor incapable—a distinct middle state.

The Mu'tazila proposed a novel view: when an action occurs, the agent is in a state that is neither capacity nor incapacity. This challenges the common assumption that one must be either capable or incapable at any given moment. In this passage, the author presents their argument and its philosophical elaboration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the two distinct parts: the initial Mu'tazilite claim and the later philosophical expansion.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two reasons given for why the agent is neither capable nor incapable: (1) capability would require ability to leave the act, which is impossible; (2) incapability would prevent the act, yet it occurs.
3
Understand the three states
The philosopher elaborates that the human has three states (existence, possibility, impossibility) and that the state of non-existence encompasses possibility and impossibility. See how this leads to the state of existence being distinct from capacity and incapacity.
4
Synthesize the argument
Combine the proofs and the three-state analysis to see the full Mu'tazilite position: the act's existence defines a unique state that is neither capacity nor incapacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام للمعتزلة حالة وجود الفعل حالة وسط بين حالة القدرة وحالة العجز] ثم إن الكلام في جانب الفعل - وهو أن حصوله لما جاز مع عدم القدرة في الثاني جاز مع عدمها في العاشر لاستوائهما في العدم - حمل بعض المعتزلة على أن زعم أن القدرة وإن انعدمت في الثاني فالفاعل لن يصير في تلك الحال موصوفًا بالعجز ولن يبقى موصوفًا بالقدرة، فالفعل يحصل ممن ليس بقادر ولا عاجز، ولن يتصور وجود الفعل إلا ممن هذا وصفه، وزعم أن القادر على الفعل قادر على تركه، وترك الموجود مستحيل فلم يكن هو حال وجود الفعل قادرًا لأنه ليس بقادر على تركه، لأن تركه مستحيل فلا يدخل تحت القدرة، وزعم أن العاجز غیر موجود منه ما هو عاجز عنه ، وهذا وجد منه الفعل فلم يكن قادرًا ولا عاجزاً. وهي حالة ذكر هذا بعض من ينسب إلى الفلسفة (وهي العلم) بحقائق الأمور والوقوف على دقائق العلوم، ونسب مخالفيه في ذلك إلى العدول عن سبيل المعارف، وطول فيه وهول، وزعم أن الفعل له حالتان حالة عدم وحالة وجود ولا واسطة بينهما، والإنسان له أحوال ثلاثة وهي حالة وجود الفعل منه وحالة إمكانه وحالة امتناعه، وإحدى حالتي زمن الفعل تستغرق حالتين من الأحوال الثلاثة الأخر عدم الفعل، فإن حالة عدم الفعل تستغرق حالة امتناع الفعل من الإنسان وحالة إمكانه منه إذ الفعل ممن الفعل عنه ممتنع معدوم وكذا ممن الفعل منه ممكن، لأن الممكن أن يكون هو الممكن أن لا يكون، والفعل الموجود غير ممكن أن لا يكون، فالممكن إذا ما يكون معدومًا بعد، فإذا كانت إحدى حالتي الفعل وهي حالة العدم تستنفد هاتين الحالتين - أعني إمكان الفعل وامتناعه فقد تبين إذا أن الحالة الثالثة من الأحوال مقابلة للحالة الباقية من الحالتين - أعني أن الوجود مقابل الوجود – ثم حالة الامتناع حالة العجز ، وحالة الإمكان حالة القدرة، فتبقى حالة وجود الفعل لا بمقابلة حالة القدرة لفوت صفة الإمكان عن الموجود، وحالة القدرة حالة الإمكان لا حالة الوجود ، ولا بمقابلة حالة الامتناع التي هي حالة العجز لأن من يمتنع عنه الفعل يستحيل وجوده منه، وقد وجد في هذه الحالة فلم يكن
Speech of the Mu'tazila: The state of existence of the act is a middle state between the state of capacity and the state of incapacity. Then, the discussion concerning the side of the act—that its occurrence, since it is possible with absence of capacity in the second [time], is possible with its absence in the tenth because of their equality in non-existence—led some of the Mu'tazila to claim that even if capacity is absent in the second [time], the agent will not in that state become described as incapable, nor will he remain described as capable. So the act comes about from one who is neither capable nor incapable, and the existence of the act is inconceivable except from one who has this description. And he claimed that the one capable of the act is capable of leaving it, but leaving what exists is impossible, so he is not, in the state of the act's existence, capable, because he is not capable of leaving it, since leaving it is impossible and thus does not fall under capacity. And he claimed that the incapable person does not produce from himself what he is incapable of, but here the act came from him, so he is neither capable nor incapable. And this state—some of those who attribute themselves to philosophy (which is knowledge of the realities of things and grasping the subtleties of sciences) mentioned it, and he attributed his opponents in that to deviating from the path of knowledges, and he went on at length and made it formidable. And he claimed that the act has two states: a state of non-existence and a state of existence, with no intermediary between them. And the human has three states: the state of the act's existence from him, the state of its possibility, and the state of its impossibility. And one of the two states of the time of the act encompasses two of the three states—the other being the non-existence of the act. For the state of non-existence of the act encompasses the state of impossibility of the act from the human and the state of its possibility from him, since the act from one from whom the act is impossible is non-existent, and likewise from one from whom the act is possible, because the possible may be and may not be, while the existent act is not possible not to be. So the possible is at times still non-existent. So when one of the two states of the act—namely the state of non-existence—exhausts these two states, i.e., the possibility and impossibility of the act, then it becomes clear that the third state among the states corresponds to the remaining one of the two states—i.e., existence corresponds to existence. Then the state of impossibility is the state of incapacity, and the state of possibility is the state of capacity. So the state of existence of the act remains: it does not correspond to the state of capacity because the attribute of possibility is missing from the existent, and the state of capacity is the state of possibility, not the state of existence; nor does it correspond to the state of impossibility, which is the state of incapacity, because from one from whom the act is impossible, its existence from him is inconceivable. Yet it has occurred in this state, so he was not...
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite argument for a middle state (hala wast) between capacity (qudra) and incapacity ('ajz). The argument originates from the earlier claim that if action can occur at time two without capacity, it can also occur at time ten without capacity. This leads some Mu'tazila to assert that during the act, the agent is neither described as capable nor incapable. They reason: (1) If the agent were capable, he would be capable of leaving the act, but leaving the existent is impossible, so he is not capable. (2) If the agent were incapable, he would not produce what he is incapable of, but he does produce the act, so he is not incapable. Therefore, the agent is in a third state. The author then attributes this view to a philosopher who claims that the act has only two states (existence and non-existence) with no intermediary, but the human has three states (existence, possibility, impossibility). This philosopher then argues that the state of non-existence of the act encompasses both possibility and impossibility, leaving the state of existence as distinct from both capacity and incapacity. He concludes that the state of existence does not correspond to capacity (since capacity is about possibility, not actual existence) nor to incapacity (since the act exists). The argument ends abruptly, possibly incomplete, but it clearly delineates the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms
حالة
القدرة
العجز
الإمكان
الامتناع
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila deny capacity entirely.
Correction: They do not deny capacity; they posit a third state that is neither capacity nor incapacity during the act.

Checkpoint
Why does the Mu'tazilite argument claim the agent is not capable during the act?
A) The agent has no power to act.
B) The agent cannot leave the existing act, so capability to leave is absent.
C) The agent is actually incapable.
D) Capacity is irrelevant to the act.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argued that during the act, the agent is neither capable nor incapable, but in a distinct middle state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that during the very moment you commit a sin, you are not capable of leaving it. Reflect on a time you persisted in a wrong action. Did you feel trapped, as if you couldn't stop? How does this argument challenge the common belief that 'you can always stop if you want'? What does this mean for your reliance on Allah's help before entering such a state?
?
If during the act you are neither capable nor incapable, then what does that say about your accountability? Are you excused? Or does the state itself demand a careful preparation beforehand? Examine your own habits: do you take precautions before moments of weakness, or do you assume you can always 'just stop'?
?
The Mu'tazila base their argument on the impossibility of leaving an existing act. Consider your daily prayers: when you are in the middle of salah, are you capable of leaving it? Does that moment feel like a state of 'being in the act' that is distinct from before and after? How can this reflection deepen your presence (khushu') during prayer?

Refutation of the claim that power endures until action
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim of enduring power by posing a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that power endures until the act by showing that it leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Power cannot endure because that would require either an act without its own power or an enduring accident—both impossible.

In this passage, al-Nasafi takes on a Mu'tazilite attempt to salvage their view that power exists before the act and endures. He first notes that enduring accidents have already been disproven. Then he grants the hypothesis hypothetically and poses a dilemma that forces the opponent into contradiction. The argument is a masterclass in dialectical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it, then read the English translation. Let the structure of the argument come through.
Notice the use of conditional 'if' statements that set up the dilemma.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find the sentence where the Mu'tazila state that power exists before the act and endures until the act. This is the view being refuted.
The claim is: القدرة توجد قبل الفعل، ثم تبقى إلى وقت وجود الفعل
3
Follow the dilemma
Al-Nasafi asks a key question: if power endures, is it power for the same act that occurs with it, or for a different act? Trace both branches of the dilemma.
Each branch leads to a problem: either the act occurs without its own power, or power exists without an object.
4
See the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that the opponent's position collapses into either accepting the endurance of accidents (already refuted) or affirming a power with no object (absurd).
This shows the power of dialectical argument in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحمل هذا الكلام بعض المعتزلة على أن ركبوا المحال، وجوزوا بقاء القدرة وقالوا: القدرة توجد قبل الفعل، ثم تبقى إلى وقت وجود الفعل فلا(٤) يكون الفعل ثابتا ممن ليس بقادر.
وقد أقمنا الدلالة على بطلان القول ببقاء الأعراض فلم يمكنهم التعلق بهذا المحال، ثم نقول : هب أن القدرة تبقى إلى وقت وجود الفعل ولكن مع هذا لا ينفعكم ذلك فإنا نقول لكم : إذا بقيت إلى وقت وجود (الفعل) (٥) أكانت حالة البقاء قدرة على هذا الفعل الذي اقترن (۱) بها وقت البقاء ؟ أم كانت قدرة لا على هذا الفعل بل لفعل غيره يفعل بها في الثاني من وقت وجود هذا الفعل؟
فإن قالوا: هي في حالة البقاء قدرة على فعل آخر غير هذا الذي وجد في حال بقاءه وقدرة هذا الفعل هي فى أول (٢) أحوال وجودها ويتعقبها الفعل، ثم هي في الثاني في حال وجودها قدرة فعل آخر يوجد في الثالث من حال وجودها وهو قول بعضهم.
:قلنا هذا مثل الأول فإنها لما لم تكن فى كل حال قدرة لما يقارنها من الفعل بل هي قدرة على ما يتعقبه من الفعل كان كل فعل ثابتًا لا بما له من القدرة، ووجود ما هو قدرة على غيره لا يجدي نفعا ولا يدفع ما ألزمنا. وإن قالوا: هي في الثاني قدرة على ما ثبت فيه من الفعل المقارن فيها.
قلنا: إذا كانت هي في الثاني قدرة على ما يثبت فيه من الفعل فكانت هي قدرة (الفعل الذي اقترن بها، وبطل جميع سعيهم، واضمحل أكثر شبهاتهم، ولم يحصل لهم من القول بتقدمها ) (۳) على زمان وجود الفعل - مع أنها في الحال المتقدمة على وجود الفعل ليست بقدرة لهذا الفعل (٤) - إلا القول ببقاء الأعراض وهو باطل، والقول بوجود قدرة لا مقدور لها ولا مقارناً لها ولا متأخرا عنها، وهو محال.
And some Mu'tazila interpreted this speech by compounding the absurd, and they allowed the endurance of power and said: Power exists before the act, then it remains until the time of the act's existence, so the act is not established from one who is not powerful (٤). And we have established the proof of the falsity of the statement on the endurance of accidents, so they cannot cling to this absurdity. Then we say: Suppose that power remains until the time of the act's existence, but with this, that does not benefit you. For we say to you: When it remains until the time of the existence (of the act) (٥), is it, in the state of endurance, a power for this act that is concomitant (۱) with it at the time of endurance? Or is it a power not for this act but for another act that is done by it in the second [moment] from the time of this act's existence? If they say: In the state of endurance it is a power for another act other than this one that exists in the state of its endurance, and the power of this act is in the first (٢) of the states of its existence and the act follows it, then in the second it is, in the state of its existence, a power for another act that exists in the third of the states of its existence—and this is the statement of some of them. We say: This is like the first, for since it is not in every state a power for the act that is concomitant with it, but rather it is a power for what follows it, then every act is established not by its own power, and the existence of what is a power for something else yields no benefit and does not repel what we have necessitated. And if they say: In the second it is a power for what is established therein of the concomitant act. We say: If it is in the second a power for what is established therein of the act, then it is a power (for the act that is concomitant with it, and all their effort is nullified, and most of their doubts vanish, and they gain nothing from the statement of its precedence) (۳) over the time of the act's existence—while it is, in the state preceding the act's existence, not a power for this act (٤)—except the statement on the endurance of accidents, which is false, and the statement on the existence of a power that has no object of power (maqdur), neither concomitant with it nor posterior to it, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite attempt to salvage their position by claiming that power endures from before the act until the act. Al-Nasafi first notes that the doctrine of enduring accidents has already been proven false. He then considers the hypothesis hypothetically: if power endures, does it remain as a power for the same act that occurs with it, or for a different act? If it is for a different act, then the act in question occurs without its own power, which is problematic. If it is for the same act, then power is indeed existent with the act at the second moment, but then the power that preceded was not power for this act. This forces the opponent to accept either that power exists without an object, or that accidents endure—both are false. Thus the position collapses.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
بقاء (endurance)
أعراض (accidents)
مقدور (object of power)
فعل (act)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Nasafi accepts the possibility of enduring power for the sake of argument, but he uses it only to show its absurd consequences.
Correction: Al-Nasafi does not endorse the premise; he uses it hypothetically to expose the logical contradiction in the opponent's view.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does al-Nasafi pose to those who claim power endures?
A) Either power is eternal or created.
B) Either power remains for the same act or for a different act.
C) Either power is inherent or accidental.
D) Either power is before the act or after the act.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's refutation shows that the Mu'tazilite claim of enduring power leads to logical contradictions, thus it cannot be true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila clung to their claim despite its absurdity. Is there a belief or habit in your life that you hold onto even when it leads to inner contradiction? Will you examine it honestly today and, if needed, let it go?
?
Al-Nasafi's argument shows that each act requires its own power at the very moment of occurrence. Do you live as if your actions are disconnected from the present moment? How can you become more mindful that each act is a fresh gift from Allah, requiring His power?
?
If power cannot endure, then your ability to act is renewed every instant. Does this awareness change how you prepare for prayer, work, or relationships? What will you do today to rely on Allah's moment-by-moment power rather than your own assumed continuity?
