The Mu'tazila View on Human Actions
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila view that humans create their own actions, including its historical development.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine of human action creation and its historical evolution from cautious terminology to extreme claims.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila progressively escalated their claim that humans create their own actions, eventually denying Allah's creative role.

In this passage, Nasafi outlines the Mu'tazila position on human actions, showing how their views evolved over time. Early Mu'tazila were cautious, but later figures like Abu Ali al-Jubbai and al-Ja'd took more extreme positions. By tracing this development, we see the sect's departure from mainstream Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall narrative. Notice the key figures and terminology shifts.
Focus on the historical progression mentioned.
2
Identify the three stages
The passage describes three phases: the early Mu'tazila, Abu Ali al-Jubbai, and al-Ja'd. Identify what each group/individual claimed about human action and divine creation.
Early Mu'tazila avoided calling the servant a 'creator'; al-Jubbai did; al-Ja'd denied Allah's true creation.
3
Examine the terminology
Note the shift from using 'ijad' and 'ihdath' (invention/origination) to 'takhliq' (creation). Understand why this was significant.
The term 'creator' (khalik) is reserved for Allah in mainstream Islam.
4
Reflect on the author's tone
Observe how Nasafi criticizes the Mu'tazila, especially al-Ja'd. This shows the gravity of the divergence from consensus.
The phrase 'exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say' underscores the condemnation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعضهم جعلوها العباد وقطعوا تدبير الله عنها بالكلية، وقالوا: يخترعها العباد فيخرجونها من العدم إلى الوجود ويحدثونها، ويقولون: إيجادها وإحداثها شاء الله تعالى ذلك أم لم يشأ، وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة القدرية بأجمعهم، إلا أن أوائلهم كانوا يتحرجون عن إطلاق لفظة التخليق وتسمية العبد خالقا (1)، وكانوا يطابقون جميع المسلمين في قولهم: لا خالق إلا الله وإن كانوا يثبتون معنى الخلق كانوا يطلقون لفظة الإيجاد والإحداث، ويسمون العبد موجدًا محدثًا إلى زمن أبي علي الجبائي، فلما رأى معنى الخلق ثابتًا أطلق لفظة الخالق (1) وسمي العبد خالقًا، ولم يبال من مخالفة إجماع الأمة مع إقراره بكونه حجة موجبة للعلم قطعًا كالنص لما رأى من تحير وعجزهم عن التفرقة بين الإيجاد والتخليق، وكان الناس يتعجبون من جرأته على الله تعالى وركوبه حطة (2) مخالفة إجماع المسلمين ليتمكن من تحقيق ضلالاته إلى أن نشأ أبو عبد الله (3) البصري (4) المعروف بالجعد منهم وأتى بما لم يتجاسر عليه مشرك، فزعم أن الله تعالى ليس بخالق فى الحقيقة بل يوصف بذلك مجازا وإنما الخالق على الحقيقة هو العبد - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرًا (6).
And some of them attributed [actions] to the servants and completely cut off Allah's management from them, and said: the servants invent them, bringing them from non-existence to existence, and cause them to occur, and say: their invention and occurrence [happen] whether Allah wills that or not. And the Mu'tazila Qadariyya all went to this, except that their early ones were reluctant to using the term 'creation' and calling the servant a creator (1), and they agreed with all Muslims in their saying 'there is no creator but Allah', even though they affirmed the meaning of creation; they would use the term 'invention' and 'origination', and call the servant an inventor and an originator until the time of Abu Ali al-Jubbai. When he saw that the meaning of creation is established, he used the term 'creator' (1) and called the servant a creator, and he did not care about opposing the consensus of the Ummah while acknowledging that it is a proof necessarily producing knowledge like the text, because he saw their confusion and inability to distinguish between invention and creation. And people were astonished at his audacity against Allah and his undertaking a great matter (2) of opposing the consensus of Muslims in order to realize his misguidances, until Abu Abdullah (3) al-Basri (4) known as al-Ja'd arose among them and presented what no polytheist had dared: he claimed that Allah is not a creator in reality but is described as such metaphorically, and the actual creator is the servant – exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, greatly (6).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila position on human actions. The author begins by stating that some people attribute actions entirely to the servants, cutting off Allah's management. They claim humans invent actions independently, whether Allah wills or not. The author notes that all Mu'tazila Qadariyya held this view, but with a historical evolution: the early ones avoided the word 'creation' (takhliq) and calling the servant a creator, instead using 'invention' (ijad) and 'origination' (ihdath). They called the servant an inventor and originator. This changed with Abu Ali al-Jubbai, who saw the meaning of creation as established and therefore used 'creator' for the servant, disregarding the consensus of the Ummah. The author criticizes this audacity. Then al-Ja'd went further, claiming that Allah is not truly a creator but only metaphorically, and the real creator is the servant. The author ends with a condemnation.

Correction: The passage shows an evolution: early Mu'tazila were cautious and avoided calling the servant a creator, while later figures like Abu Ali al-Jubbai and al-Ja'd increasingly deviated.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view on human actions developed from cautious terminology to outright denial of Allah's creative role, with al-Ja'd claiming Allah is only metaphorically creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Al-Ja'd claimed Allah is only metaphorically the creator—an extreme few would openly say. But do you ever, in moments of pride or self-reliance, act as if you are the true creator of your achievements? What verse or hadith will you recite whenever you feel that tendency arise?

Mu'tazila's Use of 'Khaliq' and al-Ja'd's Extremism
Historical narrative of Mu'tazila's attribution of creation to humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical progression of Mu'tazila thought on attributing creative power to humans, from cautious terms to the extreme claim that the servant is the true creator.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila escalated from 'originator' to 'creator' for humans; al-Ja'd claimed only servants create.

This lesson traces a troubling development within Mu'tazila theology. What began as cautious language about human agency ended with an explicit claim that Allah is not the real creator. We will follow this shift from early terms to al-Jubba'i's bold step, and finally to al-Ja'd's extreme assertion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the historical narrative. Pay attention to the specific terms used.
2
Identify the stages of escalation
Notice the progression: earlier Mu'tazila used 'mujid' and 'muhdith'; al-Jubba'i then called the servant 'khaliq'; al-Ja'd went further by denying that Allah is truly the creator.
3
Consider the violation of consensus
The text says al-Jubba'i disregarded ijmā' despite acknowledging it as a definitive proof. Ask yourself why he would do this and what it reveals about his methodology.
4
Reflect on al-Ja'd's extreme claim
Al-Ja'd claimed that Allah is not the real creator but only metaphorically, and that the true creator is the servant. This is a radical departure from orthodox Islamic belief.
The passage ends with a Quranic condemnation.
5
Connect to orthodox response
The author concludes with the Quranic verse, showing the gravity of such statements. Consider why the consensus of the Ummah is important in preserving correct belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لفظة الإيجاد والإحداث، ويسمون العبد موجدًا محدثًا إلى زمن أبي علي الجبائي، فلما رأى معنى الخلق ثابتًا أطلق لفظة الخالق وسمي العبد خالقًا، ولم يبال من مخالفة إجماع الأمة مع إقراره بكونه حجة موجبة للعلم قطعًا كالنص لما رأى من تحير وعجزهم عن التفرقة بين الإيجاد والتخليق، وكان الناس يتعجبون من جرأته على الله تعالى وركوبه حطة مخالفة إجماع المسلمين ليتمكن من تحقيق ضلالاته إلى أن نشأ أبو عبد الله البصري المعروف بالجعد منهم وأتى بما لم يتجاسر عليه مشرك، فزعم أن الله تعالى ليس بخالق فى الحقيقة بل يوصف بذلك مجازا وإنما الخالق على الحقيقة هو العبد - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرًا.
The word of bringing into existence and originating, and they called the servant a bringer into existence and an originator until the time of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i. When he saw the meaning of creation established, he released the word 'creator' and called the servant a creator, and he did not care about violating the consensus of the Ummah while acknowledging it as a definitive proof like the text, because he saw their confusion and inability to differentiate between bringing into existence and creation. And people were amazed at his audacity against Allah and his undertaking a grave act of violating the consensus of Muslims to be able to realize his misguidances, until there arose Abu Abd Allah al-Basri known as al-Ja'd from among them, and he brought what no polytheist had dared: he claimed that Allah is not a creator in reality but is described as such metaphorically, and that the real creator is the servant - exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, greatly.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative tracing the radicalization of Mu'tazila thought regarding the attribution of creative power to human beings. It begins by noting that earlier Mu'tazila (before Abu Ali al-Jubba'i) used the terms 'bringer into existence' (mūjid) and 'originator' (muḥdith) for the servant, but avoided calling the servant 'creator' (khāliq). Then Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, seeing that the meaning of creation (khalq) was established in their usage, boldly applied the term 'creator' to the servant, disregarding the consensus (ijmā') of the Ummah, which he otherwise regarded as a definitive proof (ḥujja) equivalent to a text (naṣṣ). The author expresses amazement at his audacity. The narrative culminates with Abu Abd Allah al-Basri (known as al-Ja'd), who went even further: he claimed that Allah is not a creator in reality but only metaphorically, and that the true creator is the servant. The passage ends with a Quranic condemnation of such statements, showing the author's rejection of this extreme view.

إحداث

خلق
تخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: This passage shows a progression of extremism: earlier Mu'tazila used cautious terms, then al-Jubba'i called the servant 'khaliq', and finally al-Ja'd claimed Allah is not the real creator.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's attribution of the term 'creator' to humans escalated until al-Ja'd denied Allah's true creatorship, showing the danger of departing from consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you attribute your own achievements or abilities to yourself as the ultimate cause, forgetting that Allah is the only true Creator? In what specific areas of your life do you need to repent from this subtle shirk?
?

?
Al-Ja'd claimed Allah is not the real Creator—a statement that seems obviously false, but do you live as if you are the independent creator of your own actions and outcomes? What does the truth that 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (Quran 39:62) demand of you starting now?

First Mu'tazili Proof from 'Best of Creators'
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazili proof from 'Best of Creators' verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazili proof for human creation from the Quranic phrase 'best of creators' and the logic behind their argument.

Core Idea
A comparative phrase implies multiple participants – that is the Mu'tazili hook.

One of the most famous Quranic verses cited by the Mu'tazila to argue that humans create their own actions is the phrase 'best of creators.' In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents their reasoning before later refuting it. We will examine how they derive their proof from comparative language and the analogy they use.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Focus on understanding the structure: first, the Mu'tazila cite the verse; then, they explain why it implies other creators.
Notice the contrast with the phrase 'greatest of gods' – that is key.
2
Identify the core argument
The Mu'tazila argue that the comparative 'أحسن' (best) requires at least two parties to compare. This implies that there must be other creators besides Allah for the phrase 'best of creators' to make sense.
3
Understand the analogy and contrast
They draw an analogy with 'most skilled of writers' to illustrate how comparatives work. Then they contrast with 'greatest of gods' which is impossible because there is no other god – showing that the Quran would not use such a phrase if other creators did not exist.
This contrast strengthens their claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام بيننا وبين المعتزلة فنبدأ ببيان ما لهم من الشبه فنقول: إنهم يحتجون من كتاب الله تعالى كقوله تعالى: فَتَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَلِقِينَ، في الآية دلالة كون غيره خالقا لأن إطلاق ذلك يوجب أن له تعالى مزية على كل خالق كما يقال: فلان أحذق الكتاب وأجودهم خطًا. ألا ترى أن القول بأنه تعالى أكبر الآلهة محال لاستحالة ثبوت الإلهية لغيره، وهاهنا لما أطلق هذا دل أن غيره خالق.
As for the discourse between us and the Mu'tazila, we begin by presenting their specious arguments. We say: They argue from the Book of Allah, may He be exalted, such as His saying: 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators.' In the verse there is evidence that other than Him is a creator, because the application of this phrase necessitates that He, may He be exalted, has superiority over every creator, just as it is said: 'So-and-so is the most skilled of writers and the most excellent of them in calligraphy.' Do you not see that the statement that He, may He be exalted, is the greatest of gods is impossible due to the impossibility of divinity being established for other than Him, and here, when this was applied, it indicates that other than Him is a creator.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage begins a new section addressing the Mu'tazila. The author states that he will first present the Mu'tazila's specious arguments (شبه) before refuting them. The first proof they cite is from Quran 23:14: 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (فَتَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَلِقِينَ). The Mu'tazila argue that this phrase implies there are other creators, because the comparative 'best' (أحسن) requires multiple participants—otherwise one would not say 'best' if there were only one. They draw an analogy: if you say 'So-and-so is the most skilled of writers and the most excellent of them in calligraphy,' this implies there are other writers. Similarly, 'best of creators' implies there are other creators besides God. They contrast this with a phrase like 'the greatest of gods' (أكبر الآلهة), which would be impossible because there is no other god; thus such a phrase is not used. Here, since God used 'best of creators,' it indicates that others, namely humans, are also creators. This is the Mu'tazili argument; the author is merely presenting it and will rebut it later.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الشبه
أحسن الخالقين
الخالق
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author endorses the Mu'tazili interpretation because he presents it without immediate refutation.
Correction: Imam Nasafi is first presenting their argument (shubah) before refuting it. He does not endorse it.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazila use the verse 'أحسن الخالقين' to argue for human creation?
A) They claim the verse proves God creates directly without intermediaries
B) They argue the comparative 'أحسن' implies existence of other creators
C) They say the verse negates any creation by humans
D) They interpret 'الخالقين' as angels, not humans

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use the comparative adjective in 'best of creators' to argue that other creators must exist, namely humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators,' do you feel a sense of awe that God is superior to any creator, including yourself? In what specific areas of your life do you unconsciously claim a share of creativity that belongs only to Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila used a linguistic argument to prove that humans create their own actions. If you accept that interpretation, what would that mean for your personal accountability before Allah? Are you ready to bear full responsibility for every deed as if you are its sole creator?
?
This lesson presents an argument that you may disagree with. How do you respond when the Qur'anic text seems to allow a meaning that challenges your beliefs? Do you humbly seek deeper understanding, or do you rely on surface-level readings to support your own opinions?

beginner
4 min
Mu'tazila's Linguistic Argument from 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin'
Mu'tazila use the Qur'anic phrase 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' to argue that others create

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's linguistic argument from the Qur'anic verse 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' that other beings are creators.

Core Idea
A comparative adjective implies other participants in the category.

The Mu'tazila, a rationalist theological school, often used the Qur'an itself to argue for human capacity to create actions. One of their key proofs is the verse 'So blessed be God, the best of creators' (23:14). At first glance, this verse simply praises God. But the Mu'tazila read it carefully: the word 'best' (ahsan) is a comparative, which only makes sense if there are other creators. In this lesson, we examine their reasoning and the linguistic principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Note the key phrase 'أحسن الخالقين'.
2
Identify the linguistic point
Recognize that 'ahsan' is a comparative adjective (ism tafdeel). In Arabic, a comparative implies a comparison between at least two entities.
3
Understand the analogy with 'best writer'
The Mu'tazila analogize: 'best of writers' only makes sense if there are other writers. Similarly, 'best of creators' only makes sense if there are other creators.
4
Contrast with 'greatest of gods'
They contrast this with 'greatest of gods', which is impossible because no other gods exist. This shows that the verse's form is meaningful only if other creators exist.
This is a key refutation of the Ash'ari objection that the phrase is simply hyperbolic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام بيننا وبين المعتزلة فنبدأ ببيان ما لهم من الشبه فنقول: إنهم يحتجون من كتاب الله تعالى كقوله تعالى : فَتَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَلِقِينَ في الآية دلالة كون غيره خالقا لأن إطلاق ذلك يوجب أن له تعالى مزية على كل خالق كما يقال : فلان أحذق الكتاب وأجودهم خطًا . ألا ترى أن القول بأنه تعالى أكبر الآلهة محال لاستحالة ثبوت الإلهية لغيره، وهاهنا لما أطلق هذا دل أن غيره خالق.
Now, as for the discussion between us and the Mu'tazila, we begin by explaining what doubts they have, so we say: They argue from the Book of God Almighty, such as His saying: 'So blessed be God, the best of creators' (23:14). In the verse there is evidence that other than Him is a creator, because the use of that expression necessitates that He Almighty has superiority over every creator, just as it is said: 'So-and-so is the most skillful of writers and the best of them in handwriting.' Do you not see that saying that He Almighty is the greatest of gods is impossible because divinity cannot be established for another? And here, since He used this expression, it indicates that other than Him is a creator.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Mu'tazila from the Qur'an. They cite the verse 'So blessed be God, the best of creators' (23:14). They argue that the word 'best' (أحسن) implies a comparative degree, which requires the existence of other creators over whom God is superior. They draw an analogy: if you say 'So-and-so is the most skillful of writers,' that implies there are other writers. Similarly, if it were impossible for others to create, then saying 'best of creators' would be like saying 'greatest of gods,' which is absurd because no other gods exist. Therefore, the Mu'tazila conclude that the verse proves that humans can create actions.

Key Terms
أحسن الخالقين
خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse simply praises God without implying other creators.
Correction: The Mu'tazila focus on the linguistic implication of the comparative form: a comparative adjective logically requires other members of the category to be meaningful.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazila derive the existence of other creators from the verse 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin'?
A) They argue that 'ahsan' only makes sense if there are others to compare.
B) They claim the verse explicitly states that humans create.
C) They say the verse rejects any creation by others.
D) They interpret 'khaliq' as metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that the comparative 'ahsan' in 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' necessarily implies the existence of other creators, making the verse a proof for human ability to create actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' in your prayers, do you ever pause to consider that this verse might imply other creators? How does that challenge or affirm your understanding of your own role in your actions? Will you reflect on this the next time you are tempted to claim full credit for a success?
?
If every action you perform is truly created by Allah alone, why do you feel pride or shame for your deeds? How does the Mu'tazila argument make you confront the tension between divine control and personal responsibility—and which side does your heart lean toward in moments of sin?
?
The Mu'tazila use language to prove a point. Are you using your own words and interpretations of Qur'anic verses to support your desires instead of submitting to the clear meaning? Search your heart: do you twist the text to justify your actions, or do you let the text transform you?

Mu'tazila Proof from Divine Commands and Threats
Mu'tazila argue that if God creates actions, He commands Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that if God created all human actions, divine commands, prohibitions, promises, and threats would logically apply to God Himself, leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
If God creates all acts, He commands and prohibits Himself.

The Mu'tazila present a second proof for human free will based on the nature of divine address. They argue that if God were the sole creator of human actions, then His commands and prohibitions would be directed at Himself, making Him both the commander and the commanded. This passage lays out this argument and declares that such a view leads to apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: premise, condition, consequence, conclusion.
The passage uses a conditional 'if... then' structure.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila premise
Note that the Mu'tazila start with established divine commands, prohibitions, promises, and threats directed at people for different actions.
3
Trace the logical consequence
If God were the creator of those actions (the 'if' clause), then His commands would apply to His own created acts, so He would be commanding Himself. This leads to the absurdity that God is both believer and disbeliever.
This step is crucial for the argument.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that such an implication is impossible (muhal) and whoever utters it is considered to have left the religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتعلقون أيضا بالأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد الثابتة من الله تعالى للناس في أفعال مختلفة، ولو كان الله تعالى هو الذي تولى تخليق أفعالهم وإيجادها لصار أمراً نفسه ناهيًا إياه واعدًا إياه موعدًا إياه لأنه هو الذي تولى إيجادها، فكان هو المؤمن والكافر والمطيع والعاص، وذا محال من تفوه به يحكم بانسلاخه عن الدين. وكذا الذم والمدح على ما يذم ويمدح عليه من الأفعال يكونان راجعين إليه لأن الموجد بذلك أولى ممن ليس بموجد. وكذا التعذيب والإثابة يحصلان على فعل نفسه وهما مقابلان بالفعل، فينبغي أن يكون الله تعالى هو المعاقب المثاب لا العبد، والقول به كفر.
And they also argue by command and prohibition and promise and threat established from God Almighty for people regarding different actions. If God Almighty were the one who undertook the creation and bringing into existence of their actions, then He would become a commander of Himself, a prohibitor of Himself, a promiser to Himself, a threatener to Himself, because He is the one who brought them into existence. Thus He would be the believer and the disbeliever, the obedient and the disobedient. That is impossible; whoever utters it is judged to have shed himself of the religion. Likewise, blame and praise for the actions that are blamed and praised would revert to Him, because the one who brings into existence is more deserving of that than one who does not bring into existence. Likewise, punishment and reward would occur for His own action, and they are in return for the action. Then God Almighty would be the punished and the rewarded, not the slave. And saying that is disbelief.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the second set of Mu'tazila proofs. They argue from the existence of divine commands, prohibitions, promises, and threats directed at human actions. If God were the sole creator of all human acts, then He would be commanding and prohibiting Himself, promising and threatening Himself, because He is the agent of the acts. This would lead to the absurdity that God is both the believer and the disbeliever, the obedient and the disobedient. Similarly, praise and blame for actions would logically belong to the creator of those actions, so if God creates evil acts, He would deserve blame. Likewise, reward and punishment would fall on God for His own acts. The Mu'tazila conclude that such implications are impossible and constitute disbelief.

Key Terms
الأمر
النهي
الوعد
الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine commands imply human ability; the Mu'tazila use this as a proof that humans create their own acts, otherwise commands would be directed at God.
Correction: The Mu'tazila's argument is a conditional reductio: they assume if God creates all acts, then commands apply to God, which is absurd. Therefore, He does not create all acts. The validity of the argument depends on the premise that commands cannot logically be directed to the agent performing the act if that agent is not the creator.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence do the Mu'tazila claim follows from God creating all human actions?
A) God would be both the commander and the commanded.
B) God would be the punisher and the punished.
C) Humans would have no need for prophets.
D) The Qur'an would be contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that if God were the creator of all human acts, divine commands would apply to God Himself, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that Allah creates all your actions, including your sins, then does that make you less culpable in your own eyes? Read the passage again: the Mu'tazila argue that this would make Allah the doer of sin. Are you comfortable with that implication? Search your heart: have you ever used divine predestination as an excuse for your shortcomings? Today, resolve to take full responsibility for your actions, knowing that while Allah creates, you acquire and are accountable.
?
The passage ends by declaring that anyone who says Allah creates all human actions has 'shed himself of the religion.' That is a severe charge. How does that judgment sit with your own belief in the Sunni doctrine of qadr? Do you have a clear answer that satisfies both divine omnipotence and human responsibility? If not, let this question drive you to study the works of Ahl al-Sunnah on kasb until your conviction is firm.

Mu'tazila Proof from Praise, Blame, Reward, and Punishment
Mu'tazila argue praise, blame, reward, punishment revert to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that praise and blame, as well as reward and punishment, would revert to God if He created all human actions, leading to the conclusion that God would be the one rewarded or punished.

Core Idea
If God creates all acts, He deserves praise, blame, reward, and punishment.

Having argued from commands and prohibitions, the Mu'tazila now extend the same logic to praise, blame, reward, and punishment. They claim that if God were the creator of all actions, then He would be the proper object of praise or blame for those actions, and likewise reward and punishment would fall on Him. This passage presents these consequences and declares such a view as disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice it builds on the previous argument, using 'likewise' (كذا).
This part is shorter but dense.
2
Understand the logic of praise and blame
Praise and blame are for actions. The one who brings the action into existence (al-moojid) is more deserving of praise or blame than the one who merely performs it. If God creates all actions, then praise and blame revert to Him.
3
Extend to reward and punishment
Similarly, reward and punishment are in return for actions. If God creates the action, then the reward or punishment is for His own action, so God would be the one rewarded or punished.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The passage concludes that saying God is the creator of all actions leads to God being rewarded or punished, which is disbelief (kufr).
The conclusion is very strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتعلقون أيضا بالأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد الثابتة من الله تعالى للناس في أفعال مختلفة، ولو كان الله تعالى هو الذي تولى تخليق أفعالهم وإيجادها لصار أمراً نفسه ناهيًا إياه واعدًا إياه موعدًا إياه لأنه هو الذي تولى إيجادها، فكان هو المؤمن والكافر والمطيع والعاص، وذا محال من تفوه به يحكم بانسلاخه عن الدين. وكذا الذم والمدح على ما يذم ويمدح عليه من الأفعال يكونان راجعين إليه لأن الموجد بذلك أولى ممن ليس بموجد. وكذا التعذيب والإثابة يحصلان على فعل نفسه وهما مقابلان بالفعل، فينبغي أن يكون الله تعالى هو المعاقب المثاب لا العبد، والقول به كفر.
And they also argue by command and prohibition and promise and threat established from God Almighty for people regarding different actions. If God Almighty were the one who undertook the creation and bringing into existence of their actions, then He would become a commander of Himself, a prohibitor of Himself, a promiser to Himself, a threatener to Himself, because He is the one who brought them into existence. Thus He would be the believer and the disbeliever, the obedient and the disobedient. That is impossible; whoever utters it is judged to have shed himself of the religion. Likewise, blame and praise for the actions that are blamed and praised would revert to Him, because the one who brings into existence is more deserving of that than one who does not bring into existence. Likewise, punishment and reward would occur for His own action, and they are in return for the action. Then God Almighty would be the punished and the rewarded, not the slave. And saying that is disbelief.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the second set of Mu'tazila proofs. They argue from the existence of divine commands, prohibitions, promises, and threats directed at human actions. If God were the sole creator of all human acts, then He would be commanding and prohibiting Himself, promising and threatening Himself, because He is the agent of the acts. This would lead to the absurdity that God is both the believer and the disbeliever, the obedient and the disobedient. Similarly, praise and blame for actions would logically belong to the creator of those actions, so if God creates evil acts, He would deserve blame. Likewise, reward and punishment would fall on God for His own acts. The Mu'tazila conclude that such implications are impossible and constitute disbelief.

Key Terms
الذم
المدح
التعذيب
الإثابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reward and punishment are based solely on human performance; the Mu'tazila argue that if God creates the act, the reward/punishment goes to the creator.
Correction: The Mu'tazila assume that the creator (al-moojid) is the primary subject of praise, blame, reward, and punishment. This is a key premise. Without it, the argument does not follow. The correction is to recognize that the Mu'tazila are making a logical point about agency and responsibility.

Checkpoint
What does the Mu'tazila conclude from their argument about praise, blame, reward, and punishment?
A) God is the ultimate judge of all actions.
B) If God creates all actions, He would be the one rewarded or punished.
C) Humans are entirely responsible for their own actions.
D) The prophets are the only ones who can intercede.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila conclude that if God were the creator of all human acts, He would deserve praise or blame and would receive reward or punishment, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect: In your daily life, do you ever catch yourself blaming God for a mistake you made—thinking, 'God made me do it'? How does this Mu'tazila argument challenge you to take full ownership of your actions without attributing your faults to Him? Write down one concrete situation where you are tempted to shift responsibility, and commit to saying instead: 'I chose this; I am accountable.'
?
This passage says that if God created all acts, He would be the one deserving praise or blame. When you succeed, do you genuinely praise God as the giver of ability, or do you secretly credit yourself? And when you sin, do you blame God's decree or your own choice? Be honest: which scenario makes you more uncomfortable? Why?
?
The Mu'tazila considered it 'kufr' to say that God creates all actions. Without agreeing or disagreeing, ask yourself: Do I live my life as if my deeds are ultimately my own, or as if God caused them? If I truly believed every act is from God, would I change how I seek forgiveness or how I strive to avoid sin? What would change tonight in your prayer if you took this argument seriously?

Mu'tazila's Trichotomy of Action's Origin
Mu'tazila present a trichotomy of action's origin and argue for exclusive human creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's logical trichotomy of the origin of human actions and their argument for exclusive human creation.

Core Idea
Human acts must be either from God, joint, or human—only the third escapes blame falling on God.

The Mu'tazila employ a method of induction and division to prove that humans create their own actions independently. They present three possibilities and eliminate two, leaving only the conclusion that the slave is the exclusive creator of his acts. This passage shows their logical reasoning and features a poetic summary by al-Nashi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the key terms like 'الاستقراء' (induction) and 'التقسيم' (division).
2
Identify the three divisions and the reasoning
Locate the three possible origins of the slave's act. Understand why the first two are rejected based on praise and blame.
Focus on the logical flow: if God alone creates, blame reverts to Him; if both create, blame still falls on the slave.
3
Reflect on the conclusion and the poem
Recognize that the third division (slave alone) is the only one that avoids contradiction. Consider how al-Nashi's poetic line summarizes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقرروا هذا الكلام وأبرزوه على طريقة الاستقراء واشتغلوا بالتقسيم وقالوا فعل العبد لا يخلو إما أن يكون كله من الله تعالى تولى إيجاده بلا صنع من العبد وإما أن يكون العبد أوجده أيضا مع الله تعالى فوجد بإيجاد الله تعالى وإيجاد العبد، وقد شارك العبد الله تعالى في إيجاده، وإما أن كان العبد هو الذي تولى الإيجاد على سبيل الانفراد والاستقلال والاستبداد. والقسم الأول فاسد لعود المدح والذم على هذه الأفعال إلى العبد دون الله ولو كان الله تعالى هو الموجد له لرجع إليه الذم والمدح والقسم الثاني أيضا باطل لعود كل الذم على ذلك إلى العبد دون الله تعالى، ولو كان الأمر على ذلك لم يكن العبد بأولى به من الله تعالى فتعين القسم الثالث. ونظم الناشي هذا المعنى فقال :
And they established this statement and presented it in the manner of induction and engaged in division (taqsim) and said: The act of the slave is not free from either being entirely from God Almighty, He undertakes its bringing into existence without any action from the slave; or the slave also brings it into existence together with God Almighty, so it exists by the bringing into existence of God Almighty and the bringing into existence of the slave, and the slave has shared with God Almighty in its bringing into existence; or the slave is the one who undertakes the bringing into existence in the manner of exclusivity, independence, and autonomy. The first division is invalid because praise and blame for these actions revert to the slave, not to God; and if God Almighty were the one who brought it into existence, praise and blame would revert to Him. The second division is also invalid because all blame for that would revert to the slave, not to God Almighty, and if the matter were like that, the slave would not be more deserving of it than God Almighty. So the third division is established. And al-Nashi versified this meaning and said:
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's logical trichotomy regarding the origin of human acts. They propose three possibilities: (1) God creates the act entirely without any contribution from the slave; (2) both God and the slave create it jointly; (3) the slave creates it alone, independently. They reject the first because praise and blame for actions are directed at the slave, not God. If God were the sole creator, praise or blame would logically fall on Him. They reject the second because blame still falls on the slave, not equally on God; if both create, the slave is not more deserving of blame than God. Therefore, only the third option remains: the slave creates the act independently. The poet al-Nashi composed a poem summarizing this argument.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
التقسيم
الانفراد
الاستقلال
الاستبداد
الناشي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that joint creation (second division) is plausible because both God and human could cooperate.
Correction: The Mu'tazila reject it because blame still falls on the slave alone, implying the slave is the primary cause; if God were equally the creator, the slave would not be more deserving of blame than God.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, why is the first division (God creates all actions) invalid?
A) It would make God the recipient of praise and blame.
B) It contradicts the verse 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin'.
C) It denies human free will.
D) It leads to polytheism.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's third proof uses a threefold division of action's origin to conclude that humans create their own acts independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you blame yourself for a sin, do you truly believe that you alone created that act, or do you subconsciously shift part of the blame to circumstances or even to God's decree? How does the Mu'tazila's trichotomy challenge you to take full ownership of your actions?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that if God were the sole creator, praise and blame would revert to Him. Consider your own prayers and gratitude: do you thank God for good deeds as if He created them, yet blame yourself for sins as if you created them? Is your belief consistent with the division presented?
?
Imagine you are in a situation where you are tempted to commit a sin. According to the Mu'tazila's conclusion, you are the exclusive creator of that act. Does this thought increase your sense of responsibility or does it feel like a heavy burden? How does this affect your reliance on God's help in avoiding sin?

Refuting Mu'tazila's Misuse of the Buhayrah Verse
Nasafi criticizes Mu'tazila for misinterpreting a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's criticism of the Mu'tazila's method of twisting Quranic verses from their apparent meaning to support their own theological views.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila play with God's book to fit their desires.

Nasafi points out a fundamental methodological error: the Mu'tazila take a verse that clearly speaks about entities (like the Bahirah camel) and twist it to refer to human actions. This reveals a pattern of forcing scripture into their preconceived notions rather than letting the evidence guide them. In this lesson, we examine Nasafi's critique and the logical flaw he exposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the key terms: أعيان, أفعال, اسم الفعل, محل الفعل.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's claim
What do the Mu'tazila try to prove using the Buhayrah verse? How do they apply it to human actions?
3
Understand Nasafi's counter
Nasafi argues that the verse mentions entities, not actions. See how he exposes their shift from 'noun of action' to 'locus of action'.
4
Reflect on the accusation
Nasafi accuses them of playing with God's book and following desires. Consider why this is a serious charge in theological methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتعجب منه العاقل أن المعتزلة يتعلقون في المسألة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ مَا جَعَلَ اللَّهُ مِنْ بَحِيرَةٍ ﴾ والآية، ويصرفون ذلك إلى فعلهم في هذه الأعيان، ولا شك أن المذكور في الآية أعيان لا أفعال، ثم إذا آل الأمر إلى هذا الذي هو اسم الفعل صرفوه إلى محل الفعل وبمثل هذا يتبين أنهم يلعبون بكتاب الله ويحملونه على ما تميل إليه أهواؤهم دون ما توجبه حقائق الأدلة.
And among things that astonish the rational person is that the Mu'tazila cling in the issue to His saying exalted: 'God has not made a Bahirah' and the verse, and they turn that to their action in these entities, and there is no doubt that what is mentioned in the verse are entities not actions, then when the matter comes to this which is the noun of the action, they turn it to the locus of the action, and by such it becomes clear that they play with God's book and carry it upon what their desires incline to, not upon what the realities of evidences require.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a criticism of the Mu'tazila's method of interpreting Quranic verses. The author finds it astonishing that the Mu'tazila rely on the verse 'ما جعل الله من بحيرة' (God has not made a Bahirah) to argue about human actions. He points out that the verse explicitly mentions entities (أعيان) like the Bahirah camel, not actions (أفعال). Yet the Mu'tazila twist the meaning to refer to actions. When the discussion reaches the 'noun of the action' (اسم الفعل), they reinterpret that to mean the 'locus of the action' (محل الفعل). The author accuses them of playing with God's book and following their own desires rather than the true evidence.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
أعيان
أفعال
اسم الفعل
محل الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that the verse about the Bahirah supports their view that actions are not created by God but by humans.
Correction: Nasafi points out that the verse speaks of entities (like the camel), not actions. Thus it cannot be used to argue about the nature of human actions.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi accuse the Mu'tazila of doing with the verse about the Bahirah?
A) Taking it literally as referring to actions.
B) Distorting it to support their own views.
C) Ignoring it completely.
D) Using it to prove that actions are not created.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazila's methodological error of forcing Quranic verses to mean what they want, rather than submitting to the clear text.

Refuting the Mu'tazila Using Their Own Principle
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila by using their own belief in identity of act and object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the Mu'tazila's own belief in the identity of act and object to invalidate their interpretation of the Qur'anic verse and to defend the Ash'ari/Maturidi position.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own principle that act and object are one turns against their interpretation.

In this passage, Nasafi employs a clever internal critique: he takes a core Mu'tazili doctrine—that the act (fi'l) and its object (maf'ul) are identical—and shows that it undermines their reading of the verse 'wa-Allahu khalaqakum wa-ma ta'malun' (Allah created you and what you do). By their own logic, the verse cannot refer to idols but must refer to human actions, thus supporting the Sunni position that all actions are created by Allah. Let's trace the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Mu'tazila's principle
Start by understanding that the Mu'tazila believe the act (fi'l) and its object (maf'ul) are identical. This means that when someone acts, the action itself is the very thing acted upon.
This is a key point of disagreement with Sunni theology.
2
Apply the principle to the verse
Apply this principle to the verse 'wa-Allahu khalaqakum wa-ma ta'malun'. Since 'ma ta'malun' (what you do) must be the object (ma'mul), and according to the Mu'tazila the act and object are one, the verse must refer to the action itself, i.e., the human act of making idols, not the idol.
Thus the verse condemns the act of making, not the idol itself.
3
Contrast with the carving verse
Contrast this with the verse 'a-ta'buduna ma tanhitun' (Do you worship what you carve?). Here, the carving (an-nahatu) is different from the carved (al-manhutu), so the act and object are distinct, allowing the verse to refer to the carved idol.
This difference shows that the two verses are not parallel.
4
Understand the refutation
See that by the Mu'tazila's own principle, their interpretation of the first verse as referring to idols fails. Nasafi shows that their principle forces the verse to refer to human actions, which supports the Sunni view that actions are created by Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مما يبطل التأويل على أصل المعتزلة أن عندهم الفعل والمفعول واحد فكان العمل والمعمول واحدًا، والصنم ليس بعمل للعبد فلا يكون معموله، فأما الفعل في الصنم فهو فعل العبد فكان هو بعينه معموله فانصرفت إليه الآية بخلاف قوله تعالى: أَتَعْبُدُونَ مَا تَنْحِتُونَ لأن النحت غير المنحوت، ولا اتحاد بينهما عندهم، وكان المنحوت عين الصنم، وأما فيما نحن فيه فبخلافه، والله الموفق. وبهذا يجيبهم من زعم اتحاد الفعل والمفعول ممن ساعدنا في هذه المسألة وبالله المعونة.
Then, of what invalidates the interpretation according to the principle of the Mu'tazila is that according to them the act (fi'l) and the object (maf'ul) are one, so the action (amal) and the thing acted upon (ma'mul) are one, and the idol is not an act of the servant so it is not his thing acted upon. As for the act in the idol, it is the act of the servant, so it is exactly his thing acted upon, so the verse is directed to it, unlike His saying exalted: 'Do you worship what you carve?' because carving (an-nahatu) is different from the carved (al-manhutu), and there is no identity between them according to them, and the carved is the very idol, but as for our case it is otherwise. And God is the Grantor of success. And by this he answers them who claim the identity of act and object among those who agree with us in this issue, and with God is assistance.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila based on their own principle. The author argues that according to the Mu'tazila, the act (فعل) and the object (مفعول) are identical. Therefore, the action (عمل) and the thing acted upon (معمول) are the same. Applied to the verse 'والله خلقكم وما تعملون', if 'ما تعملون' refers to the thing acted upon, then for human actions, the action itself is the thing acted upon (since they are identical), so the verse refers to human actions. However, for idols (الأصنام), the idol is not an act of the servant; the carving is the act, and the carved idol is a separate entity. Thus the verse cannot refer to idols. This is contrasted with the verse 'أَتَعْبُدُونَ مَا تَنْحِتُونَ' where the carving (نحت) is distinct from the carved (منحوت), so the verse refers to the object. The author concludes that this argument answers those who claim identity of act and object among the Ash'arites or Maturidis who agree that actions are created.

Key Terms
فعل
مفعول
عمل
معمول
اتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila think their interpretation of 'wa-Allahu khalaqakum wa-ma ta'malun' as referring to idols is consistent with their principle that act and object are identical.
Correction: Actually, their principle forces the verse to refer to human actions, not idols, because the idol itself is not an act; only the making of it is the act.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what invalidates the Mu'tazila's interpretation of the verse 'wa-Allahu khalaqakum wa-ma ta'malun'?
A) Their belief that act and object are one makes the verse refer to human actions, not idols.
B) Their belief that act and object are distinct.
C) Their denial of the literal meaning of the verse.
D) Their acceptance that actions are not created.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defeats the Mu'tazila by showing that their own doctrine of act-object identity makes the verse about human actions, not idols.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief or principle so strongly that you failed to see how it contradicted another core conviction you hold? How will you train yourself to examine your own assumptions before they lead you into inconsistency?
?
The Mu'tazila were confident in their interpretation, yet their own logic condemned them. Is there any area of your faith where you rely on a reasoning that, if applied consistently, would undermine a fundamental truth? What step will you take this week to seek correction from the Quran and Sunnah?

Refuting the Mu'tazili Proof from 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin'
Nasafi rebuts a proof using 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' from Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili proof from the verse 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' by distinguishing between two meanings of 'khalq'.

Core Idea
The verse 'Best of creators' does not prove humans create — 'khalq' can mean shaping, not inventing.

The Mu'tazila often used the Quranic phrase 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' to support their view that humans are creators of their actions. In this passage, Nasafi dismantles this proof by showing that the term 'khalq' has both a broad and a narrow sense, and that only God creates in the true sense of bringing something from nothing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the mention of two groups of Mu'tazila: early Basrians/Baghdadis and later Basrians.
The passage ends with a reference to a poet's line; focus on the argument itself.
2
Identify the first rebuttal
Notice that Nasafi first points out that early Basrians and Baghdadis agree with the Maturidis that only God is a creator. This undermines their use of the verse.
3
Examine the response to later Basrians
For those Mu'tazila who allow calling humans creators, Nasafi explains that 'khalq' in the verse means 'estimation' (taqdir) and 'forming' (taswir), not 'invention' (ikhtira') and 'origination' (ijad).
This distinction is key to the rebuttal.
4
Understand the implication
The verse does not prove that humans create actions in the real sense. It only allows a metaphorical or secondary meaning of 'khalq'.
This preserves the Maturidi position that only God is the true Creator.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Nasafi's method works: he first appeals to agreement among scholars, then clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'khalq' to resolve the apparent proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة في آية (فَتَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَالِقِينَ): ثم المتعلق من أوائل البصرية وجميع البغدادية منهم بقوله تعالى: أَحْسَنُ الْخَالِقِينَ ليس بصحيح لمساعدتهم إيانا على أن من سوى الله تعالى ليس بخالق ويستحيل وصف من سواه به، ولو تعلق بذلك متأخرو أهل البصرة المجوزون ذلك يقال لهم: الخلق يذكر ويراد به التقدير والتصوير دون الاختراع والإيجاد، وعلى هذا التقدير يجوز أن يسمى العبد خالقا قال قائلهم:
Discussion on the verse 'So blessed be God, the best of creators (ahsan al-khaliqin)': Then the adherence of the early Basrians and all the Baghdadis among them to His saying exalted: 'Best of creators' is not correct because of their agreement with us that other than God exalted is not a creator and it is impossible to describe other than Him with it. And if the later Basrians who permit that adhere to it, it is said to them: Al-khalq is mentioned and intended by it estimation (taqdir) and forming (taswir) without invention (ikhtira') and origination (ijad), and on this estimation it is permissible for the servant to be called a creator, as one of their poets said: ...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the verse 'فَتَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَالِقِينَ' (So blessed be God, the best of creators). The author notes that early Basrian Mu'tazila and all Baghdadis use this verse to argue that humans can be creators, because the word 'خالقين' (creators) is applied to God and others. However, Nasafi rebuts that this is not correct because these groups agree with the Maturidis that only God is truly a creator. For those later Basrians who do permit calling humans creators, the author explains that the word 'خلق' can mean 'estimation' (تقدير) and 'forming' (تصوير) rather than 'invention' (اختراع) and 'origination' (إيجاد). In that sense, a human can be called a creator, as a poet said. This shows that the verse does not prove that humans create actions in the true sense.

Key Terms

الخلق
التقدير
التصوير
الاختراع
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' proves that humans can be creators in the real sense.
Correction: Nasafi explains that 'khalq' in this context means estimation and forming, not creating from nothing. Only God creates in the true sense.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi explain the term 'khaliqin' in 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' to avoid admitting human creation?
A) He says it means 'creators' in the real sense, but only God is the best.
B) He says it means 'estimators' and 'formers', not inventors.
C) He denies the verse applies to God.
D) He argues that the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the Quranic phrase 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' does not imply human creation because 'khalq' can mean estimation and forming, not origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something praiseworthy, do you feel you 'created' it? How does the distinction between taqdir/taswir and ikhtira'/ijad humble your sense of ownership?
?
If 'khalq' can mean estimation and shaping, what does that imply about your role in your own deeds? Are you comfortable being merely a 'shaper' of what Allah originates?
?
The Mu'tazila wanted to protect human responsibility by attributing creation to humans. How does this passage reframe that concern? Can you accept full accountability without needing to be a creator?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Identification of Act and Object
Refutes the Mu'tazilite interpretation of a verse by distinguishing act from object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that act and object are identical leads to incoherence when applied to the Qur'anic verse about idol carving.

Core Idea
The act of carving is not the same as the carved object—a key distinction refuting the Mu'tazilite position.

This passage presents a precise theological argument. It first mentions a weaker response, then shows how the Mu'tazilite's own principle backfires when tested against a clear Qur'anic example. The verse 'Do you worship what you carve?' demonstrates that the act (carving) and the object (carved) are distinct, undermining the Mu'tazilite claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two-part structure: a weak response and a stronger refutation.
The word 'وهاء' is unclear; it may mean 'weakness'.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find where the author mentions 'some theologians' weak response and then his own refutation starting with 'then, invalidating the interpretation...'.
3
Analyze the key distinction
Focus on the verse 'Do you worship what you carve?' Why does the author use this? Because carving (نحت) is an act, and the carved (منحوت) is the object. They are not identical.
This distinction is the core of the refutation.
4
Understand the refutation
The Mu'tazilites claim act and object are one, but here the act (carving) is different from the object (carved idol). Thus their principle is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعض المتكلمين أجاب أن الآية لما احتملت الأمرين أعني أن هذه اللفظة لما كانت تذكر ويراد بها المعمول، وتذكر ويراد بها العمل حملت عليهما جميعًا، ولكن فيه وهاء لما فيه من جعل المشترك مشتملاً وهو غير سديد. ثم مما يبطل التأويل على أصل المعتزلة أن عندهم الفعل والمفعول واحد فكان العمل والمعمول واحدًا، والصنم ليس بعمل للعهد فلا يكون معموله، فأما الفعل في الصنم فهو فعل العبد فكان هو بعينه معموله فانصرفت إليه الآية بخلاف قوله تعالى: أَتَعْبُدُونَ مَا تَنْحِتُونَ ( لأن النحت غير المنحوت، ولا اتحاد بينهما عندهم، وكان المنحوت عين الصنم، وأما فيما نحن فيه فبخلافه، والله الموفق. وبهذا يجيبهم من زعم اتحاد الفعل والمفعول ممن ساعدنا في هذه المسألة وبالله المعونة.
Some theologians answered that when the verse admits two possibilities – meaning this word is used to refer to the object (المعمول) and also used to refer to the act (العمل) – it is carried upon both of them. But there is weakness in this because it involves making a homonym inclusive, which is not sound. Then, invalidating the interpretation according to the Mu'tazilite principle: with them the act and the object are one, so the work (العمل) and the object (المعمول) are identical. The idol is not an act of the covenant, so it is not its object. As for the act in the idol, it is the act of the servant, so it is precisely its object, so the verse turns to it, unlike His statement: 'Do you worship what you carve?' because carving is different from the carved, and there is no identity between them according to them, and the carved is the very idol. But in our case it is the opposite. And God gives success. And with this he responds to those who claim the unity of act and object among those who agree with us on this issue. And with God is help.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author first mentions a weak response from some theologians who say the verse can be interpreted in two ways, then refutes it. He then presents a stronger refutation based on the Mu'tazilite's own principle that act and object are identical. Using the verse 'Do you worship what you carve?' he shows that carving (act) and carved (object) are distinct. The idol is the carved object, not the act of carving. Thus the Mu'tazilite interpretation collapses because they cannot consistently apply their principle. The author concludes that this argument answers those who claim unity of act and object.

مفعول
عمل
معمول
صنم
نحت
منحوت

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act of carving is the same as the carved object.
Correction: The act and object are distinct; the verse 'Do you worship what you carve?' shows carving (نحت) is not the carved (منحوت).

Key Takeaway

The Mu'tazila Interpretation and Its Dismissal
Presents and dismisses the Mu'tazila interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila interpretation of Qur'an 67:14 and why Imam al-Nasafi declares it corrupt and an annulment of the verse.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila interpretation, though grammatically possible, strips the verse of its threatening context.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by laying out the Mu'tazila's grammatical reinterpretation of the verse 'Does He not know who created?' He then swiftly declares it corrupt and annulling. Let's see how he grounds this strong verdict.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting how the Mu'tazila shift the grammatical role of 'man' (who) from subject to object.
The Mu'tazila offer two possible readings, both making Allah the subject of 'knows' but differing on the agent of 'created'.
2
Identify the author's verdict
Find the sentence where the author declares the interpretation corrupt and an annulment. Notice the strong language: 'fāsid' and 'ibṭāl'.
The author does not engage in lengthy refutation here; he simply brands it as annulment.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why is it so important to reject this interpretation? What would be lost if it were accepted?
This will be answered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار لهم أيضا إلى ما هو المعقول في المسألة نصرة من الله تعالى لحزبه وتأييدًا لدينه بكلا نوعي الحجة أعني بهما السمعي والعقلي، والله تعالى يحق الحق ويبطل الباطل ولو كره المجرمون. وتأويل المعتزلة أن كلمة "من" ليست براجعه إلى الله تعالى وليست هي الفاعلة بفعل يعلم بل هي مفعولة والفاعل مضمر وهو اسم الله تعالى، وتقدير الآية ألا يعلم الله من خلق ثم بعد ذلك يحتمل أن يكون قوله: خلق، فعلاً لله تعالى واقعًا على كلمة "من" فيكون تقدير الآية: ألا يعلم الله من خلقهم، ويحتمل أن يكون قوله: خلق، فعلا لمن فيكون تقدير الآية: ألا يعلم الله من خلق القول وأسره وجهر به، هذا تأويل فاسد بل هو إبطال الآية، فإن الآية وردت مورد التوعد كقوله تعالى: ﴿اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ إِنَّهُ بِمَا تَعْمَلُونَ بَصِيرٌ﴾ أي أن الله عليم بأقوالكم أسررتم بها أو جهرتم، غير أنه خص المضمر من القول بالعلم لما أن إثبات العلم به إثبات للعلم بما جهر به منه ضرورة وإثبات العلم بذلك إثبات المجازات كما في قوله: ﴿اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ﴾ وذا ظاهر في مبتذل الكلام ثم خرج قوله: ﴿أَلَا يَعْلَمُ مَنْ خَلَقَ﴾ مخرج تقدير العلم بذلك على ما قررنا، ولذا خرج عقيب إثبات العلم به، كما يقال: إنه عالم بأخلاق فلان ومذاهبه وطرائقه وضرابته، ألا يعلم من صحبه مدة عمره وطول حياته؟ ثم ليس في إثبات العلم بمن خلق الله تعالى إثبات للعلم بما لم يخلقه وإن كان فعلاً له، كما ليس في إثبات تخليق الأنفس إثبات تخليق أفعالها عند المعتزلة، فيصير قوله: ﴿أَلَا يَعْلَمُ مَنْ خَلَقَ﴾ خاليًا عن الفائدة خارجًا على حكم العبث – جل الله عمن يكون هذا وصف كلامه.
And He also pointed out to them what is reasonable in the issue, as a victory from Allah Almighty for His party and a support for His religion with both types of proof, namely the transmitted and the rational. And Allah Almighty establishes the truth and abolishes falsehood, even if the criminals dislike it. And the interpretation of the Mu'tazila is that the word 'man' (who) does not refer to Allah Almighty, and it is not the agent of an act that He knows, but rather it is the object, and the subject is implicit, which is the name of Allah Almighty. And the estimation of the verse is: 'Does not Allah know whom He created?' Then after that, it is possible that His saying 'khalaqa' (created) is an act of Allah Almighty occurring upon the word 'man', so the estimation would be: 'Does not Allah know those whom He created?' And it is possible that His saying 'khalaqa' is an act of 'man', so the estimation would be: 'Does not Allah know who created the saying, its secret and its public?' This is a corrupt interpretation; rather, it is an annulment of the verse. For the verse came in the context of threat, like His saying: 'Do what you will; indeed, He is Seeing of what you do.' That is, Allah is Knowing of your sayings, whether you conceal them or make them public. However, He specified the inner thought from speech with knowledge, because establishing knowledge of it is necessarily establishing knowledge of what is made public from it, and establishing knowledge of that is establishing impossible things, as in His saying: 'Do what you will.' And this is apparent in common speech. Then His saying: 'Does He not know who created?' came out in the form of estimating knowledge of that according to what we have established. Therefore, it came out after the establishment of knowledge of it, just as it is said: 'He knows the character of so-and-so, his ways, his methods, and his associates; does he not know who accompanied him for the duration of his life and the length of his existence?' Then there is not in establishing knowledge of 'whom Allah created' an establishment of knowledge of what He did not create, even if it is an act of His, just as there is not in establishing the creation of souls an establishment of the creation of their actions according to the Mu'tazila. So His saying: 'Does He not know who created?' becomes devoid of benefit, falling under the rule of frivolity – exalted is Allah above the one whose speech is such.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation of the Qur'anic verse 'ألا يعلم من خلق' (Qur'an 67:14). The author first notes that Allah supports His party with both transmitted and rational proofs. Then he explains the Mu'tazila claim: the word 'man' (who) is not the subject but the object, with an implicit agent 'Allah', and they offer two possible readings. The author declares this interpretation corrupt and an annulment of the verse. He argues that the verse is a threat, similar to 'اعملوا ما شئتم', and that the context requires that Allah's knowledge includes even inner thoughts. He then uses an analogy: just as one who knows a person's character is expected to know his companions, so the verse implies that Allah's knowledge of the created includes their actions. He concludes that the Mu'tazila interpretation would make the verse meaningless and frivolous, which is unbecoming of Allah's speech.

Key Terms
تأويل
المعتزلة
إبطال الآية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila interpretation is a legitimate grammatical alternative that does not affect the meaning.
Correction: The author shows it destroys the verse's purpose as a threat, making it meaningless.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Nasafi consider the Mu'tazila interpretation to be an 'ibṭāl' (annulment) of the verse?
A) Because it violates Arabic grammar.
B) Because it removes the verse from its context of threat and makes it frivolous.
C) Because it denies that Allah knows all things.
D) Because it was invented by heretics.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila interpretation is corrupt because it annuls the threatening implication of the verse.

?

?
If you truly believed that Allah knows every secret and that His threats are real, what would you change about how you live tonight?

The Context of Threat and the Analogy
Provides a positive argument and analogy to refute Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi proves that the verse 'Ala ya'lamu man khalaq' is a threat and uses an analogy to support the correct interpretation.

Core Idea
The verse's context of threat requires Allah's knowledge to encompass even inner thoughts.

Having dismissed the Mu'tazila reading, Imam al-Nasafi now builds a positive argument. He places the verse in the category of threat verses and uses a powerful analogy to show that knowledge of the created implies knowledge of their actions. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the threat context
Read how the author compares this verse to 'Do what you will; indeed, He is Seeing of what you do.' Notice the parallel structure.
Both verses imply that Allah knows even hidden actions.
2
Study the analogy
The author gives an analogy: if someone knows a person's character deeply, he is expected to know who accompanied him. Apply this to Allah's knowledge of creation.
The analogy shows that knowledge of the created naturally includes knowledge of their actions.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Read the final sentence: if the Mu'tazila interpretation were true, the verse would be devoid of benefit and frivolous. Understand why that conclusion follows.
This reinforces the earlier verdict of annulment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار لهم أيضا إلى ما هو المعقول في المسألة نصرة من الله تعالى لحزبه وتأييدًا لدينه بكلا نوعي الحجة أعني بهما السمعي والعقلي، والله تعالى يحق الحق ويبطل الباطل ولو كره المجرمون. وتأويل المعتزلة أن كلمة "من" ليست براجعه إلى الله تعالى وليست هي الفاعلة بفعل يعلم بل هي مفعولة والفاعل مضمر وهو اسم الله تعالى، وتقدير الآية ألا يعلم الله من خلق ثم بعد ذلك يحتمل أن يكون قوله: خلق، فعلاً لله تعالى واقعًا على كلمة "من" فيكون تقدير الآية: ألا يعلم الله من خلقهم، ويحتمل أن يكون قوله: خلق، فعلا لمن فيكون تقدير الآية: ألا يعلم الله من خلق القول وأسره وجهر به، هذا تأويل فاسد بل هو إبطال الآية، فإن الآية وردت مورد التوعد كقوله تعالى: ﴿اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ إِنَّهُ بِمَا تَعْمَلُونَ بَصِيرٌ﴾ أي أن الله عليم بأقوالكم أسررتم بها أو جهرتم، غير أنه خص المضمر من القول بالعلم لما أن إثبات العلم به إثبات للعلم بما جهر به منه ضرورة وإثبات العلم بذلك إثبات المجازات كما في قوله: ﴿اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ﴾ وذا ظاهر في مبتذل الكلام ثم خرج قوله: ﴿أَلَا يَعْلَمُ مَنْ خَلَقَ﴾ مخرج تقدير العلم بذلك على ما قررنا، ولذا خرج عقيب إثبات العلم به، كما يقال: إنه عالم بأخلاق فلان ومذاهبه وطرائقه وضرابته، ألا يعلم من صحبه مدة عمره وطول حياته؟ ثم ليس في إثبات العلم بمن خلق الله تعالى إثبات للعلم بما لم يخلقه وإن كان فعلاً له، كما ليس في إثبات تخليق الأنفس إثبات تخليق أفعالها عند المعتزلة، فيصير قوله: ﴿أَلَا يَعْلَمُ مَنْ خَلَقَ﴾ خاليًا عن الفائدة خارجًا على حكم العبث – جل الله عمن يكون هذا وصف كلامه.
And He also pointed out to them what is reasonable in the issue, as a victory from Allah Almighty for His party and a support for His religion with both types of proof, namely the transmitted and the rational. And Allah Almighty establishes the truth and abolishes falsehood, even if the criminals dislike it. And the interpretation of the Mu'tazila is that the word 'man' (who) does not refer to Allah Almighty, and it is not the agent of an act that He knows, but rather it is the object, and the subject is implicit, which is the name of Allah Almighty. And the estimation of the verse is: 'Does not Allah know whom He created?' Then after that, it is possible that His saying 'khalaqa' (created) is an act of Allah Almighty occurring upon the word 'man', so the estimation would be: 'Does not Allah know those whom He created?' And it is possible that His saying 'khalaqa' is an act of 'man', so the estimation would be: 'Does not Allah know who created the saying, its secret and its public?' This is a corrupt interpretation; rather, it is an annulment of the verse. For the verse came in the context of threat, like His saying: 'Do what you will; indeed, He is Seeing of what you do.' That is, Allah is Knowing of your sayings, whether you conceal them or make them public. However, He specified the inner thought from speech with knowledge, because establishing knowledge of it is necessarily establishing knowledge of what is made public from it, and establishing knowledge of that is establishing impossible things, as in His saying: 'Do what you will.' And this is apparent in common speech. Then His saying: 'Does He not know who created?' came out in the form of estimating knowledge of that according to what we have established. Therefore, it came out after the establishment of knowledge of it, just as it is said: 'He knows the character of so-and-so, his ways, his methods, and his associates; does he not know who accompanied him for the duration of his life and the length of his existence?' Then there is not in establishing knowledge of 'whom Allah created' an establishment of knowledge of what He did not create, even if it is an act of His, just as there is not in establishing the creation of souls an establishment of the creation of their actions according to the Mu'tazila. So His saying: 'Does He not know who created?' becomes devoid of benefit, falling under the rule of frivolity – exalted is Allah above the one whose speech is such.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation of the Qur'anic verse 'ألا يعلم من خلق' (Qur'an 67:14). The author first notes that Allah supports His party with both transmitted and rational proofs. Then he explains the Mu'tazila claim: the word 'man' (who) is not the subject but the object, with an implicit agent 'Allah', and they offer two possible readings. The author declares this interpretation corrupt and an annulment of the verse. He argues that the verse is a threat, similar to 'اعملوا ما شئتم', and that the context requires that Allah's knowledge includes even inner thoughts. He then uses an analogy: just as one who knows a person's character is expected to know his companions, so the verse implies that Allah's knowledge of the created includes their actions. He concludes that the Mu'tazila interpretation would make the verse meaningless and frivolous, which is unbecoming of Allah's speech.

مبتذل الكلام
العبث

Misconception: The Mu'tazila interpretation preserves the verse's meaning while adjusting grammar.
Correction: The author proves it annuls the verse's threatening context, making it pointless.

Key Takeaway
The correct understanding of Q. 67:14 is that Allah's knowledge encompasses all that He creates, including their inner thoughts and actions.
