Why the Mu'tazila View Leads to Tashbih and Its Refutation
Argument that Mu'tazila view leads to tashbih and refutation of Ka'bi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila definition of act as 'bringing from non-existence to existence' leads to problematic similarity (tashbih) between divine and human acts, and how the author refutes al-Ka'bi's objection by distinguishing essential from relative attributes.

Core Idea
Acts similar in essence cannot be differentiated by relative moral qualities.

In this passage, the author argues forcefully that the Mu'tazila definition of act as 'bringing from non-existence to existence' makes God's acts and human acts essentially identical—a form of tashbih. When al-Ka'bi objects that human acts involve submission or frivolity while God's acts are wisdom, the author counters by showing that such qualities are relational, not essential. This lesson walks through the argument and its refutation, clarifying a key Maturidi distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Absorb the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse quoted.
2
Identify the core claim and the objection
The author claims the Mu'tazila view necessitates similarity; al-Ka'bi objects that moral differences prevent similarity. Find these two parts.
3
Trace the refutation
Follow how the author distinguishes between the essence of an act (e.g., motion) and its relative attributes (obedience, submission). Note the examples given.
The terms 'asma' idafiyya' (relative names) are key.
4
Analyze the examples
Look at the blackness of Zayd/'Amr and the rest vs. motion examples. How do they illustrate that similarity in essence is unaffected by accidental differences?
5
Reflect on the theological outcome
Consider why this argument matters: it preserves God's transcendence while still acknowledging human acts have a real nature.
The Mu'tazila, by defining act as ikhraj, inadvertently made divine and human acts essentially the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول المعتزلة يؤدي إلى التشابه بين فعل الله وفعل العبد: وإنما قلنا: إنه يؤدي إلى وقوع التشابه بين فعل القديم وفعل الحادث، فإن فعل الله تعالى لما كان إخراجًا من العدم إلى الوجود، وفعل العبد كذلك، كان كل واحد من الفعلين شبيه صاحبه خصوصا على أصلهم فإن عندهم الفعل والمفعول واحد فحركة المرتعش فعل الله ومفعوله وحركة العبد فعل العبد ومفعوله، فلا فرق بين حركة وبين حركة بمعنى من المعاني فكان فعل العبد وخلقه كخلق الله تعالى وقد قال الله تعالى: أَمْ جَعَلُوا لِلَّهِ شُرَكَاءَ خَلَقُوا كَخَلْقِهِ، فَتَشَبَه الخَلْقُ عَلَيْهِمْ وهذا، والله أعلم استفهام بمعنى الإنكار والنفي، فكان الله تعالى نافيًا أن يكون من أحد خلق كخلقه. فإن قالوا: إن فعلنا وإن كان إخراجًا من العدم إلى الوجود، وفعله تعالى أيضا إخراج من العدم إلى الوجود إلا أنه لا تشابه بين فعلنا وفعله، لأن فعلنا خضوع وذلة أو عبث وفساد وفعله عز وجل حكمة وصواب وليس بخضوع ولا ذلة، والعبث لا يشبه الحكمة. هذا هو اعتراض الكعبي. يقال له: إن الحركة حركة لنفسها، وكذا السكون والسواد والبياض، وكون الفعل طاعة أو معصية أو ذلة أو خضوعاً لمعاني وراء الذات، فإن الفعل يكون طاعة لخروجه على موافقة الأمر، والمعصية تكون معصية لخروج الفعل على مخالفة الأمر وارتكاب ما نهى عنه، فكانا من الأسماء الإضافية، وكذا الحركة لا تكون ذلة لنفسها، وكذا لا تكون خضوعا لنفسها بل لقصد فاعلها، والقصد معنى في القلب والحركة تكون بالجوارح والمشتبهان لذاتيهما لا يختلفان باعتبار الأسامي الإضافية الثابتة لمعاني هي أغيار للذات. ألا ترى أن سواد زيد وسواد عمرو لا يختلفان لاختلاف المحل، وسكون زيد لا يكون مشابها لحركته وإن كان الفاعل واحدًا لاختلافهما في ذاتيهما، فإذا لم يكن لاتحاد الفاعل أثر في تشابه ما اختلفا بذاتيهما لا يكون لاختلاف الفاعل أو معنى من معانيه أو اختلاف آلة الفاعلين أثر في اختلاف شيئين اشتبها لذاتيهما، وهذا لأن
The statement of the Mu'tazila leads to similarity between the act of God and the act of the servant. And we only say that it leads to the occurrence of similarity between the act of the Eternal and the act of the originated. For the act of God, exalted, is a bringing forth from non-existence to existence, and the act of the servant is likewise. Thus each of the two acts is similar to its counterpart, especially according to their [the Mu'tazila's] principle, for according to them the act and the effect are one. So the motion of a trembling person is God's act and His effect, and the motion of a servant is the servant's act and his effect. There is no difference between one motion and another motion in any sense. So the act and creation of the servant is like the creation of God, exalted. And God, exalted, said: 'Or have they attributed to God partners who created like His creation, so that the creation became similar to them?' And this, God knows best, is an interrogative of denial and negation. So God, exalted, negated that anyone could have a creation like His creation. If they say: 'Our act, though it is a bringing forth from non-existence to existence, and His act, exalted, is also a bringing forth from non-existence to existence, yet there is no similarity between our act and His act, because our act is submission and abasement or frivolity and corruption, while His act, mighty and majestic, is wisdom and rightness, not submission or abasement. Frivolity does not resemble wisdom.' This is the objection of al-Ka'bi. It is said to him: Motion is motion in itself, and likewise rest, blackness, and whiteness. The act being obedience or disobedience, or abasement or submission, is due to meanings beyond the essence. For the act becomes obedience due to its accordance with the command, and disobedience becomes disobedience due to the act's violation of the command and commission of what was forbidden. So these are among relative names (asma' idafiyya). Similarly, motion is not abasement in itself, nor is it submission in itself, but rather due to the intention of its agent. Intention is a meaning in the heart, and motion occurs by the limbs. Two things that are similar in their essences do not differ by virtue of relative names attached to meanings that are other than the essence. Do you not see that the blackness of Zayd and the blackness of 'Amr do not differ due to difference of subject, and the rest of Zayd is not similar to his motion, even if the agent is one, due to their difference in essences? So if the unity of the agent has no effect in making similar two things that differ in their essences, then the difference of the agent or a meaning among its meanings or the difference of the instrument of the agents has no effect in differentiating two things that are similar in their essences. This is because...
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an argument that the Mu'tazila's position leads to tashbih (anthropomorphism/similarity) between God's acts and human acts. The author begins by asserting that the Mu'tazila view necessitates similarity because both divine and human acts are defined as bringing forth from non-existence to existence. According to the Mu'tazila, act and effect are one, so the motion of a trembling person (caused by God) and the voluntary motion of a servant are identical in essence. The author cites Quran 13:16, which denies that any creature creates like God's creation, using it against the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila (represented by al-Ka'bi) object that acts differ in moral qualities: human acts are submission or frivolity, while God's acts are wisdom. Thus they deny similarity. The author refutes this by distinguishing between the essence of an act and its accidental attributes. An act like motion is, in itself, just motion. Qualities like obedience, disobedience, submission, or wisdom are relative (idafiyya) and depend on factors like intention and command. Since two things similar in essence do not differ due to relative attributes (e.g., two black objects remain similar in blackness regardless of the subject), the mere difference in moral quality does not negate the essential similarity of the acts as acts of bringing into existence. The author gives examples: blackness of two individuals is the same despite different subjects; rest and motion differ in essence, not by agent. Therefore, the Mu'tazila objection fails.

Key Terms
تشابه (tashabuh)
فعل (fi'l)
إخراج من العدم إلى الوجود (ikhraj min al-'adam ila al-wujud)
أسماء إضافية (asma' idafiyya)
كعبي (Ka'bi)
حركة (haraka)
ذلة (dhilla)
خضوع (khudu')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that moral differences (e.g., submission vs. wisdom) between divine and human acts prevent any similarity, so the Mu'tazila view is safe from tashbih.
Correction: The author shows that such moral qualities are relative (idafi) and do not affect the essential nature of the act. Acts similar in essence (both are 'bringing into existence') remain essentially similar regardless of these relative attributes.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute al-Ka'bi's claim that human acts (submission, frivolity) are essentially different from divine acts (wisdom)?
A) By arguing that submission and wisdom are both relative attributes that do not change the essence of the act.
B) By denying that human acts involve bringing from non-existence to existence.
C) By claiming that the Mu'tazila do not actually believe in such differences.
D) By citing a different Quranic verse that affirms the similarity.

Key Takeaway
The essential nature of an act (e.g., bringing into existence) does not change with moral qualities, which are relative and depend on intention and command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you attribute it to your own power or to God's creation? How does the Mu'tazila error of equating the essence of acts challenge your sense of humility? Will you consciously remind yourself that your ability to act is a gift from God?
?
The passage says intention (qasd) determines the moral quality of an act. Examine your own intentions: are your acts truly for God's sake, or are they tainted by seeking recognition? What concrete step can you take today to purify your intention?
?
If God's act and human act are similar in essence (both bring into existence), how does that make you view your own creative actions? Do you ever feel like you are 'creating' something independently? How can you keep alive the awareness that true creation is only from God?

The Najjariyya Principle and Essential Similarity of Temporal Things
Nasafi presents the Najjariyya principle and applies it to similarity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Najjariyya view that similarity is intrinsic, and why Nasafi affirms essential similarity among temporal things despite accidental differences.

Core Idea
Two temporal things share the same essence of origination; accidental differences do not affect their similarity.

This passage opens with a key principle of the Najjariyya school: similar things are similar by themselves, not by an added meaning. Nasafi first uses this to argue that the Creator and creatures are not similar, then shows that temporal things agree in the essence of origination. An analogy with blackness illustrates that accidental qualities do not remove essential similarity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Najjariyya principle
Read the first sentence carefully. What does the Najjariyya believe about similarity?
They believe similarity is intrinsic, not by an added meaning.
2
Distinguish Creator and creatures
Notice how Nasafi applies the principle to the Creator: because the Creator is knower by Himself and creatures are not, there is no similarity.
3
Understand essential similarity among temporal things
Focus on the argument that two originated things share the same meaning of origination (ḥudūth), and differences like effort do not change that.
The blackness analogy reinforces that accidental properties do not affect essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أصل النجارية المشتبهان مشتبهان بأنفسهما لا باشتباه هو معنى وراء المشتبهين والباري عالم بنفسه وليس المحدث عالما بنفسه، فلا يكون بينهما مشابهة على أصلهم، فأما المحدثان فلم يكونا ثم كانا فاتفقا بأنفسهما في معنى الحدوث، وقول القائل: أحدهما حدث لا بعلاج ولا تعب، والآخر حدث بعلاج وتعب، ليس بنفي الاشتباه عن المحدثين بأنفسهما إذ ليس الحدوث إلا الوجود من العدم، وهذا المعنى لا يختلف في حق حادث وحادث وإن كان أحدهما حدث بعلاج وتعب والآخر حدث لا بعلاج وتعب، إذ العلاج والتعب معنى وراء نفس الحادث ونفي معنى وراء نفس الحادث وإثبات ذلك المعنى في حق حادث لا يوجب تغيرا في ذاتيهما.
ألا ترى أن من قال: هذا سواد لزيد، وهذا سواد ليس لزيد لا الاختلاف بين السوادين ولا زوال الاشتباه، فكذا هذا.
ذلك يوجب
فإن قالوا: إن قولنا: حركة اسم عام والاشتباه يكون بالصفة الخاصة، وكونها طاعة ومعصية صفة خاصة في الحركة، لأنها تتنوع إلى الطاعة والمعصية. قلنا: قد مر إبطال هذا في مسألة التشبيه، ثم نقول: إن كون الحركة طاعة أو معصية ليس براجع إلى الذات بل ذلك اسم ثبت لورود الأمر به، فلا تغير به الصفة الراجعة إلى الذات لأن الذات لا تتغير به.
ألا ترى أن ضد الحركة وهو السكون قد يوصف بهما.
والذي يحقق هذا: اشتباه سواد زید بسواد، عمرو، ولا يقال السواد اسم عام
And according to the principle of the Najjariyya, the two similar things are similar by themselves, not by a similarity that is a meaning beyond the two similar things. And the Creator is knower by Himself, while the temporal thing is not knower by itself, so there is no similarity between them according to their principle. As for the two temporal things, they were not and then were, so they agree by themselves in the meaning of origination. And the statement of one who says: One of them came into being without treatment or effort, and the other came into being with treatment and effort, does not negate similarity between the two temporal things in themselves, because origination is only existence after non-existence, and this meaning does not differ in the case of two originated things, even if one came into being with treatment and effort and the other without, because treatment and effort are a meaning beyond the originated thing itself, and negation of a meaning beyond the originated thing and affirmation of that meaning in the case of an originated thing does not necessitate change in their essences. Do you not see that if one says: This is blackness belonging to Zaid, and this is blackness not belonging to Zaid, there is no difference between the two blacknesses nor removal of similarity. So likewise this. That necessitates. Then they said: Our saying 'movement' is a general name, and similarity is by the particular attribute, and its being obedience or disobedience is a particular attribute in the movement, because it is divided into obedience and disobedience. We said: The refutation of this has already passed in the issue of tashbih. Then we say: That movement is obedience or disobedience does not refer back to the essence, but rather that is a name established by virtue of the command regarding it, so the attribute referring to the essence does not change by it, because the essence does not change by it. Do you not see that the opposite of movement, which is rest, may be described by both? And that which confirms this: the similarity of Zaid's blackness and Amr's blackness, and it is not said that blackness is a general name.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage continues the argument by first citing the Najjariyya view that similarity is intrinsic, not by an added meaning. Nasafi uses this to distinguish between the Creator and creatures: the Creator is knower by Himself, while a temporal thing is not knower by itself, so no similarity. For two temporal things, they share the essence of origination (al-ḥudūth) – existence after non-existence. Accidental differences like treatment and effort do not affect this essential similarity; they are extra meanings that do not change the essence. An analogy: two instances of blackness remain similar in essence even if one is owned by Zaid and the other not. Then an objection: the opponent says 'movement' is a general name and similarity is by the particular attribute (being obedience or disobedience). Nasafi responds that this was already refuted in the discussion of tashbih (anthropomorphism). He reiterates that obedience/disobedience is a legal designation (due to command), not an essential attribute; the essence remains unchanged. He gives an example: rest (the opposite of movement) can be described as both obedience and disobedience, showing that the attribute is accidental. The passage ends with the same analogy of blackness to confirm the point.

Key Terms
نجارية
حدوث
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if two acts differ in accidental qualities like effort, they cannot be essentially similar.
Correction: Nasafi argues that essential similarity in origination is unaffected by such accidents; they are extra meanings beyond the essence.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why do two temporal things remain similar even if one comes into existence with effort and the other without?
A) Because effort is part of the essence of origination
B) Because origination (ḥudūth) is the same meaning in both
C) Because effort does not exist in temporal things
D) Because both are created by the same agent

Key Takeaway
Essential similarity in origination is not affected by accidental differences like effort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at others who have achieved much with ease, do you secretly devalue their deeds? Can you see in them the same origin from non-being that you share, and honor that essential similarity as a gift from Allah?
?
Your own deeds—whether done with great effort or effortlessly—share the identical reality of origination. Has your pride in your hard work made you overlook that even the easiest action is a creation from nothing? Commit to seeing every deed, easy or hard, as a direct act of Allah’s bringing forth from non-existence.
?
The analogy of blackness reminds you that ownership does not change essence. You may own your actions, but their reality as temporal things is identical to the actions of others. Is there someone whose good deeds you dismiss because of their different circumstances? Will you now regard their deeds as equally originating from Allah, and remove any trace of spiritual arrogance from your heart?

Answering the Objection: Movement and Accidental Attributes
Nasafi responds to an objection about movement and accidental attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's response to the objection that 'movement' is a general name and similarity is based on particular attributes like obedience and disobedience.

Core Idea
Obedience and disobedience are legal designations, not essential attributes; they do not affect the essence of movement.

The opponent argues that 'movement' is a general term and similarity is determined by specific attributes such as obedience or disobedience. Nasafi refutes this by appealing to a previous argument, then explaining that these attributes are merely names given by command, not essential qualities. He uses the example of rest, which can be both obedience and disobedience, and the blackness analogy to show that the essence remains unchanged.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the opponent's claim: 'movement' is a general name, and similarity is by the particular attribute of being obedience or disobedience.
This sectarian objection likely comes from a group that denies essential similarity based on such attributes.
2
Understand Nasafi's response
Nasafi first says the refutation has already passed in the discussion of tashbih. Then he clarifies: obedience/disobedience do not refer back to the essence; they are names established by the command.
3
Reflect on the examples
Note the example of rest (opposite of movement) being described as both obedience and disobedience. Then the blackness analogy confirms that the general name 'blackness' does not negate similarity.

?

?

Refuting Mu'tazila: The Error of Restricting Power to Invention
Accuses Mu'tazila of ignorance for deeming possible things impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's inability to conceive power beyond invention leads them to deem possible things impossible, and how this reflects ignorance.

Core Idea
Deeming a possible thing impossible is ignorance; deeming the necessary impossible is worse.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi directly attacks the Mu'tazila for a fundamental conceptual error. They restrict the relationship between divine power and human acts to the aspect of invention (ikhtira'), wrongly concluding that two powers cannot attach to one act. The author argues that this shows their ignorance, as they fail to consider other aspects like acquisition. By the end, we see how this mistake leads them to deny what is rationally possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's rhetorical style come through.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the key argument is complete.
2
Identify the accusation
The author begins by addressing the Mu'tazila directly. He says whoever deems a possible thing impossible is ignorant (jahil), and then strengthens: deeming the necessary impossible is even worse. Notice the escalation.
The term 'necessary' here refers to what is rationally necessary, e.g., the permissibility of two powers on one act from different aspects.
3
Pinpoint the specific error
The Mu'tazila's error is that they only conceive power's attachment to acts from the aspect of invention (ikhtira'). Their opponents agree that two powers cannot both be inventive on one act, but the real discussion is about one power being inventive and another acquisitive.
The text cuts off, but the argument is clear: the Mu'tazila conflate the aspects, leading them to deny the possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك وقد وقعت الخصومة فيه ثم ما أقمنا من الدلائل موجب ذلك ومن عد الممكن ممتنعا فهو جاهلي فكيف بمن عد الواجب ممتنعا؟ ثم هم إنما عدوا ذلك ممتنعا لأنهم لم يعقلوا تعلق القدرة بالمقدور إلا من جهة الاختراع وذلك لعمري محال وعند خصومهم قط لا يجوز تعلق قدرتين بجهة الاختراع بمقدور واحد وإنما الكلام وراء ذلك وهو أن قدرة واحدة لما تعلقت
So it was said to them: Why did you say that, while the dispute has occurred in it, and we have established proofs that necessitate it? And whoever deems the possible impossible is ignorant, so how about one who deems the necessary impossible? Then they only deemed that impossible because they did not conceive the attachment of power to the act of power except from the aspect of invention, and that, by my life, is impossible. And according to their opponents, it is absolutely not permissible for two powers to attach to one act of power from the aspect of invention; rather, the discussion is beyond that—namely, that one power when it has attached...
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of the Mu'tazila. The author rhetorically asks them why they hold their position despite the proofs presented. Calling someone who deems a possible thing (mumkin) impossible (mumtani') ignorant, the author intensifies the criticism: how much worse to deem a necessary thing (i.e., the permissibility of two powers on one act) impossible? The root of their error is that they only understand the relationship of power to the act of power from the aspect of invention (ikhtira'), which indeed would be impossible. But their opponents agree that two powers cannot attach from the aspect of invention. The real discussion is about one power attaching from the aspect of invention and another from acquisition. The text cuts off, but the argument is clear: the Mu'tazila have confounded the aspects.

واجب
تعلق القدرة
جهة الاختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is saying two powers can both be creative, but he clarifies that the issue is about different aspects (creative vs. acquisitive).
Correction: The author agrees with the Mu'tazila that two powers cannot both be inventive, but argues that one can be inventive and the other acquisitive, which is possible.

Checkpoint
What is the main error the author attributes to the Mu'tazila in this passage?
A) They deny that God creates acts.
B) They only conceive power as relating to acts through invention, ignoring acquisition.
C) They reject the consensus of the scholars.
D) They claim that a single act can be created by two creators.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's error is restricting divine power to the aspect of invention, leading them to incorrectly deem impossible what is actually possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila deemed something impossible simply because they could not conceive of it. Where in your life have you rejected a possibility because it didn't fit your limited understanding of Allah's power? Will you now ask Him to expand your heart to trust in what He can do beyond your imagination?
?
The author calls those who deem the possible ignorant. Are you quick to label as 'impossible' of Allah what you have not seen or experienced? Today, identify one matter you have been despairing of—will you repent and say, 'Allah is able to do all things,' even if it requires a paradigm shift in how you think about His power?
?
This lesson reveals that the root of error is a narrow conception of divine action. What current beliefs or habits in your worship (or daily life) might be based on a restrictive view of how Allah acts? Will you study the divine names and attributes more deeply to correct this, beginning now?

Refutation of Bishr's Stone Division
Refutation of Bishr's stone division attempt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's attempt to solve the problem of two agents moving a stone by dividing its parts is inconsistent with the assumption of equal agents.

Core Idea
Dividing the stone's parts doesn't solve the problem—it just shifts the inconsistency.

The Mu'tazilite doctrine of tawallud led to a dilemma: if two equal agents move a stone, the movement is one act attributable to both. Bishr tried to escape by dividing the stone's parts. In this passage, the author shows that Bishr's solution fails when the agents are assumed equal in all respects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author sets up the problem with the Ahl al-Sunna's forced argument.
2
Identify the key moves
The passage has three parts: (1) the Ahl al-Sunna's ilzām about two agents moving a stone, (2) Bishr's response dividing the stone's parts, and (3) the refutation focusing on equality of agents. Find each part.
3
Analyse Bishr's attempted solution
Bishr tries to avoid one act for two agents by splitting the stone's parts. Why does he think that helps? What does he say about even vs. odd parts?
Consider the assumption that agents are equal in all respects.
4
Understand the refutation
The author's interlocutor presses Bishr: if the agents are equal, how can one produce more movement? Bishr concedes equality is impossible. This shows the division only works if you abandon the initial assumption of equality.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
This is a classic kalam argument pattern: a forced consequence (ilzām) and an attempted escape that leads to another inconsistency. Think about why this method is effective in theological debate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then they, with their refusal of the attachment of two powers to one thing within power from two aspects, and the consensus of all rational people on the impossibility of their attachment to it from the aspect of creation, led them their corrupt principles to permit their attachment to it from one aspect, namely creation. So the matter became grave for them, and their leaders dispersed in seeking a way out, but they could not, and they only obtained ignorance and departure from the testimonies of cognitions. And that is because the Ahl al-Sunna forced upon them, according to their statement about tawallud (generation), that if two men equal in power moved a stone and the stone moved, then what was in it of movement would be an act of both movers, a thing within power for both. So one thing would be an act of two agents, a thing within power for two powerful agents. Thus what they directed against the Ahl al-Sunna of denying a thing within power for two powerful agents, and against the theologians of the Ahl al-Hadith and al-Najjariyya of the impossibility of one thing being an act of two agents, is invalidated. Then the Mu'tazila divided in repelling this forcing (ilzām). Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir claimed that if the parts of the stone were even, half of it would move by one and the other half by the second. It was said to him: If the parts were odd, for example eleven parts? He said: Six movements from it belong to one and the five remaining to the second. It was said to him: If they were equal in build, strength, energy, and will, how can it be conceived that more is attributed to one and less to the other? He said: It is impossible that they be equal in that. So it was said to him: Why did you say that? He said: Because if they were equal in that, it would necessitate one of two things: either the existence...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite attempt to escape a necessary consequence of their own doctrine of tawallud (generation). The author first notes that the Mu'tazila, despite their refusal to allow two powers on one act from different aspects, were forced by their own principles to permit two powers from one aspect (creation) in the case of two agents moving a stone. This forced them into inconsistency. The Ahl al-Sunna presented a dilemma: if two equal agents move a stone, then the movement is one act attributable to both, which would mean one thing in power for two agents. This invalidates the Mu'tazila's denial of such a possibility. In response, the Mu'tazila split, and Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir attempted to resolve it by dividing the stone's parts: if the number of parts is even, each moves half; if odd, one moves more than the other. The author's interlocutor then presses Bishr: if the two agents are exactly equal in all respects, it is inconceivable that one would produce more movement than the other. Bishr concedes that equality in that is impossible, but the text ends with his unfinished argument that equality would necessitate one of two things (presumably absurdities).

إلزام

زوج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dividing the stone's parts completely resolves the problem of one act for two agents.
Correction: The division only works if agents are unequal; but the original scenario assumed equality, so the solution fails.

Checkpoint
How does Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir attempt to avoid the conclusion that one stone movement can be an act of two agents?
A) He denies that the stone moves at all.
B) He claims the stone's parts are moved by different agents.
C) He says the agents are never truly equal.
D) He invokes the concept of kasb.

Key Takeaway
Bishr's division of the stone's parts fails because it requires agents to be unequal, contradicting the initial assumption of equality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult argument that challenges your deeply held beliefs, do you find yourself making minor adjustments (like Bishr dividing the stone) to avoid the full force of the critique, rather than honestly reconsidering your entire position? Can you think of a specific instance and what it cost you intellectually or spiritually?
?
The passage reveals that even a clever escape fails if the underlying assumptions are inconsistent. Is there an area of your faith or life where you are holding onto a position that requires you to ignore or deny an obvious inconsistency? What would it cost you to admit the inconsistency and seek the truth, even if it means changing your view?
?
Bishr was forced to admit that equality of agents is impossible to avoid contradiction. Are there truths about Allah's attributes—His oneness, power, or justice—that you find uncomfortable because they seem to conflict with your human reasoning? How do you respond when your reasoning leads to a conclusion that feels difficult or mysterious?

Contradiction in Accident Division: Speech and Endurance
Exposing a contradiction in accident division regarding speech and endurance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inconsistency in applying division by place/time/agent to some accidents but not to divine speech or enduring accidents.

Core Idea
Accidents must be divided consistently or not at all—no exceptions for speech or endurance.

The author exposes a logical contradiction in his opponent's view of accident division. The opponent claims that accidents divide by place, time, and agent—except for divine speech and enduring accidents, which he treats as immune to such division. This inconsistency undermines the opponent's overall position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify where the opponent's contradiction is stated.
The passage begins with 'Then he contradicts himself' – this sets the tone.
2
Identify the two points of contradiction
First, the opponent divides accidents by places but claims divine speech is one in many places. Second, he asserts that most accidents endure over two times but does not make time difference cause difference.
The author uses the opponent's own criteria against him.
3
Reflect on the logical force
Ask yourself: Why is this a contradiction? If division by place/time/agent is a general rule, it must apply to all accidents, including speech and enduring ones.
This exposes arbitrariness in the opponent's system.
4
Consider the broader implication
This contradiction shows that the opponent's principles are not consistently applied. It strengthens the author's own position on the unity and simplicity of divine attributes.
For example, divine speech (kalām) is eternal and not subject to spatial division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه ناقض حيث زعم أن الأعراض تنقسم باعتبار الأمكنة والأزمنة والفاعلين فيكون ما وجد منها في مكان غير ما وجد في غيره من الأمكنة، وما وجد منها في زمان غير ما وجد منها في غيره من الأزمنة. ثم زعم أن الكلام الواحد يحل في أماكن كثيرة متغايرة ولم يجعله متغايرا بتغاير الأمكنة، وزعم أن أكثر الأعراض توجد في وقتين لأنه يقول ببقائها، ولم يجعل تغاير الأزمنة موجبًا تغاير ما فيها من الأعراض، والله الموفق.

وزعم جعفر بن حرب المعروف بالأشج: أن حركة كل جزء منقسمة قسمين على الحقيقة نصفها لهذا الدافع ونصفها لذلك وهي ذات بعضين متغايرين حقيقة. فقيل له: ما أنكرت أن تكون الحركة التي يفعلها واحد فعلين متغايرين أيضا. أنها فعل واحد غير منقسم لانعدام علة انقسامها لأن زمانها ومكانها وفاعلها واحد.
قيل له: هذا جهل، لأن ما يتغاير في نفسه لا أثر لاتحاد الزمان والمكان والفاعل في اتحاده كما لو خلق الله تعالى في زمان واحد في جزء لا يتجزأ من الأمكنة لونًا وطعما كانا شيئين متغايرين، وإن اتحد الفاعل والمكان والزمان، فلو كانت الحركة نصفين متغايرين عند تعدد الفاعلين كانت أيضًا نصفين متغايرين عند
Then he [the opponent] contradicts [himself] when he claimed that accidents divide by consideration of places, times, and agents, so that what is found of them in one place is other than what is found in other places, and what is found of them in one time is other than what is found in other times. Then he claimed that the single speech (al-kalām al-wāḥid) subsists in many different places and did not make it differ by the difference of places. And he claimed that most accidents exist in two times because he holds their endurance (baqā'), and he did not make the difference of times necessitate the difference of what is in them of accidents. And God is the giver of success.

And Ja'far ibn Ḥarb, known as al-Ashajj, claimed: The motion of each part is divided into two halves in reality—half of it for this pusher and half for that—and it is a thing having two really distinct parts. So it was said to him: What prevents you from [saying] that the motion done by one agent is also two different acts? For it is a single indivisible act due to the absence of a cause for its division, because its time, place, and agent are one.
It was said to him: This is ignorance, because what is distinct in itself is not affected by the unity of time, place, and agent in its unity—just as if God Most High created, in one time and in an indivisible part of places, a color and a taste, they would be two distinct things, even though the agent, place, and time are one. So if motion were two different halves when there are multiple agents, it would also be two different halves when
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage contains two related refutations. First, the author exposes a contradiction (naqḍ) in the opponent's position: the opponent held that accidents divide by place, time, and agent—so that an accident in one place differs from the same accident in another place. Yet the same opponent claimed that divine speech (al-kalām) subsists in many different places without becoming different by those places. Similarly, he claimed that most accidents endure over two times (i.e., they have baqā'), yet he did not treat different times as making the accidents different. The author notes that the opponent is inconsistent: he applies division by place/time/agent to some accidents but not to speech or enduring accidents.

Second, the author refutes Ja'far ibn Ḥarb (al-Ashajj), who claimed that the motion of each part is really divided into two halves—one for each pusher—so that the motion actually consists of two distinct parts. The refutation is presented as an objection: if that were true, then why could a motion performed by a single agent not also be two distinct acts? The opponent might respond that a single agent's motion is indivisible because time, place, and agent are all one. The author retorts that this is ignorance: distinctness in essence is not affected by unity of time, place, and agent. He gives the example of God creating color and taste in one instant in an indivisible part of space: they remain two distinct things despite the unity of agent, place, and time. Therefore, even if the motion is from one agent, it could still be divisible if it were of two kinds. The argument concludes by implying that if motion can be halves for multiple agents, it can also be halves for a single agent (since unity of agent doesn't prevent essential division). The text breaks off, but the logical point is clear.

Checkpoint

A) That speech is divisible by place
B) That speech is one even though it exists in many places
C) That speech does not endure over time
D) That speech is an accident like all others

Key Takeaway
Consistency demands that the criteria for accident division apply to all accidents, including divine speech and enduring accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponent made an exception for divine speech because it is special. Are there aspects of your faith that you treat as exceptions to your usual standards—perhaps justifying them because they seem 'holy' or 'different'? How can you ensure that your understanding of God's attributes does not lead to logical contradictions in your worldview?
?
The passage ends with 'And God is the giver of success,' acknowledging that true consistency comes from divine guidance. When you find inconsistencies in your own reasoning or practice, do you turn to God for correction, or do you rely solely on your own intellect? What step can you take today to seek God’s help in aligning your beliefs and actions with the truth?

Refutation of Ja'far ibn Harb: Motion's Divisibility
Refuting Ja'far ibn Harb's claim that motion divides into real halves only with multiple agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that motion divides into real halves only with multiple agents is false, using the example of color and taste.

Core Idea
Essential distinctness does not depend on unity of time, place, or agent.

Ja'far ibn Harb argued that when two agents push an object, its motion is really divided into two halves. The author refutes this by showing that even a single agent's motion could be considered divisible if we follow the same logic. The key example of color and taste demonstrates that distinct essences remain distinct regardless of unified conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic, then the translation. Ja'far claims that motion in each part is divided into two real halves when there are two pushers.
Note the name 'Ja'far ibn Harb al-Ashajj'.
2
Understand the objection
The author objects: if motion can be two halves with two agents, why can't it be two acts with one agent? The opponent might say because time, place, and agent are one.
The objection uses reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the counter-argument and analogy
The author dismisses the opponent's response as ignorance. He gives the example of God creating color and taste in one instant at one indivisible spot—they remain distinct despite unity of agent, place, and time.
This shows that unity of conditions does not imply unity of essence.
4
Apply the conclusion to motion
If motion had distinct halves in essence, they would be distinct even with one agent, place, and time. Thus Ja'far's claim is unsound.
The argument does not require atomism; it is a logical example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

The Inconsistency of Dividing Motion by Its Cause
Al-Nasafi points out contradictions in Jafar's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inconsistency in Jafar ibn Harb's claim that motion is divided by its cause rather than in itself.

Core Idea
If motion were divided by its cause, all accidents would also divide by time and place—but Jafar contradicts himself.

Al-Nasafi begins his refutation by exposing a fatal inconsistency in Jafar's position. Jafar divides a single motion into halves based on multiple agents, yet he fails to apply the same logic consistently to other accidents and to speech. This lesson examines that initial critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Nasafi contrasts Jafar's division with the division of a part.
2
Identify the three contradictions
Al-Nasafi lists three contradictions: (1) division by cause vs. division in itself, (2) dividing accidents by place and time but not speech, (3) admitting accidents exist in two times but not dividing them.
Each contradiction undermines Jafar's consistency.
3
Reflect on the strategy
Ask yourself why al-Nasafi begins with inconsistency rather than directly refuting the division of motion. This sets up the opponent as self-contradictory.
This is a common dialectical tactic in kalam.

أنكرت أن تكون الحركة التي يفعلها واحد فعلين متغايرين أيضا. أنها فعل واحد غير منقسم لانعدام علة انقسامها لأن زمانها ومكانها وفاعلها واحد. قيل له: هذا جهل، لأن ما يتغاير في نفسه لا أثر لاتحاد الزمان والمكان والفاعل في اتحاده كما لو خلق الله تعالى في زمان واحد في جزء لا يتجزأ من الأمكنة لونًا وطعما كانا شيئين متغايرين، وإن اتحد الفاعل والمكان والزمان، فلو كانت الحركة نصفين متغايرين عند تعدد الفاعلين كانت أيضًا نصفين متغايرين عند اتحاد الفاعل، وحيث لم يكن كذلك عند اتحاد الفاعل دل أنه ليس كذلك عند تعدد الفاعلين. ثم يقال له: أيزول الجسم بنصف الحركة التي حصلها واحد منهما؟ فإن قال: نعم. فهو إذا حركه فكان وجد بكل واحد من المحركين حركة على حدة وحصل لكل محرك حركة على حدة، وهذا ليس بمذهبه. وإن قال: كل نصف ليس بحركة فإذا ليس كل نصف بزوال النصفان إذا اجتمعا فقد اجتمع ما ليس بزوال وما ليس بزوال وباجتماع ما ليس بزوال وما ليس بزوال وما ليس بحركة وما ليس بحركة لا يزول الجسم عن المكان الأول، وإذا زال دل على وجود الزوال. ثم يقال له: لو أوجد أحدهما في كل جزء من أجزاء الجسم نصف الحركة الذي هو حصته أيزول الجسم عن مكانه الأول؟ فإن قال: نعم. فهو إذا حركة على ما بينا، وإن قال: لا. فقد بقي في المكان الأول وبقي ساكنا فيه فكان فيه اجتماع السكون وبعض الحركة ويستحيل اجتماع الشيء مع بعض ما يضاده، ثم النصف الآخر أيضا ليس بحركة فيجوز أن يجتمع معه السكون أيضا إذ السكون قد يجامع ما ليس بحركة وما ليس بحركة كما يجامع اللون والطعم، وهذا كله تجاهل وخروج عن المعارف. ومقتضى هذا أن جماعة كثيرة لو حركوا جسمًا ينبغي أن تحصل حركة كل جزء متبعضة على عدد الفاعلين، فتكون كل حركة منقسمة إلى الأعشار والأسداس وأكثر، وهذا جهل فاحش.
The difference between you and those who say that the part is divisible, making what touches the earth other than what touches the sky, and what touches the north other than what touches the south, and what touches the east wind other than what touches the west wind, by analogy with your division of the single motion and the single thing upon its cause, not in itself, and this is something in which he finds no difference at all. Then he contradicts when he claims that accidents are divided with respect to places, times, and agents, so that what occurs in one place is other than what occurs in other places, and what occurs in one time is other than what occurs in other times. Then he claims that the single speech inheres in many different places and does not make it different due to difference of places, and he claims that most accidents exist in two times because he affirms their subsistence, and he does not make the difference of times necessitate the difference of the accidents in them, and God is the grantor of success. And Jafar ibn Harb, known as al-Ashajj, claimed that the motion of each part is actually divided into two halves, half belonging to this impeller and half to that, and it has two really different parts. So it was said to him: What prevents you from considering that the motion done by one agent is also two different acts? [He replied:] It is a single undivided act due to the absence of a cause for its division, because its time, place, and agent are one. It was said to him: This is ignorance, because what is different in itself, the unity of time, place, and agent has no effect on its unity, just as if God Most High created, in a single time, in an indivisible part of places, a color and a taste, they would be two different things, even though the agent, place, and time are one. So if motion were two different halves when there are multiple agents, it would also be two different halves when the agent is one, and since it is not so when the agent is one, it indicates that it is not so when there are multiple agents. Then it is said to him: Does the body depart [from its place] by the half of the motion produced by one of them? If he says: Yes. Then if he moves it, then each of the two movers would have a separate motion, and each mover would obtain a motion separately, and this is not his doctrine. If he says: Every half is not a motion, then not every half is a departure; when the two halves combine, then that which is not a departure and that which is not a departure have combined, and by the combination of what is not a departure and what is not a departure and what is not a motion (and what is not a motion does not cause the body to depart) the body does not depart from its first place, and if it does depart, it indicates the existence of departure. Then it is said to him: If one of them produced in each part of the parts of the body the half of the motion that is his share, does the body depart from its first place? If he says: Yes. Then it is a motion as we explained. If he says: No. Then it remains in the first place and remains static in it, so there would be the combination of rest and some motion, and it is impossible for a thing to combine with some of its opposite. Then the other half is also not a motion, so it is possible for rest to combine with it as well, since rest may accompany what is not a motion and what is not a motion, just as it accompanies color and taste. All this is feigned ignorance and departure from knowledge. And the consequence of this is that if a large group moved a body, the motion of each part would become fragmented according to the number of agents, so that each motion would be divided into tenths, sixths, and more, and this is egregious ignorance.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim made by Jafar ibn Harb (known as al-Ashajj) that motion is genuinely divided into two halves when multiple agents act. The refutation proceeds in several steps. First, al-Nasafi points out the contradiction in the opponent's position: the opponent divides a single motion by reference to its cause (the agent) rather than in itself, yet he himself divides accidents by place, time, and agent. He then notes that the opponent inconsistently treats speech as one despite multiple places, and accidents as remaining over two times without dividing them. After establishing this inconsistency, al-Nasafi presents Jafar ibn Harb's specific view: the motion of each part is actually two halves, each belonging to a different impeller, and the two halves are really different. The refutation then asks: If a single agent produces motion, why would it not also be two different acts? The opponent replies that motion from one agent is one and undivided because time, place, and agent are unified. Al-Nasafi calls this ignorance and gives a counterexample: God can create a color and a taste in the same indivisible place at the same time, and they remain two different things despite unified agent, place, and time. Therefore, the unity of agent, place, and time does not necessitate unity of the act. So if motion were two halves when agents are multiple, it would also be two halves when the agent is one—but since it is not so for one agent, it is not so for multiple agents. Next, al-Nasafi poses a dilemma about whether the body departs from its place by half of the motion. If the opponent says yes, then each mover would produce a separate motion, which contradicts his doctrine. If he says no, then the half is not a motion, so combining two non-motions cannot cause departure; but if departure occurs, it must come from something else. Then another dilemma: if one mover produces half the motion in each part, does the body depart? If yes, then it is a motion (which contradicts the claim that it is only half). If no, then the body remains static, combining rest with some motion—impossible because motion and rest are opposites. Moreover, the other half is also not a motion, so rest can coexist with it, just as rest coexists with color and taste. Al-Nasafi concludes that this entire position is feigned ignorance and a departure from true knowledge. He further shows that the view leads to absurdity: if many agents move a body, the motion would be divided into as many parts as agents, leading to infinite division (tenths, sixths, etc.), which is clearly false.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ (indivisible part)
حركة (motion)
عرض (accident)
متغاير (different/diverse)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dividing a motion by its cause (agent) is a consistent way to analyze motion.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that this leads to contradictions when applied to other accidents, such as speech and time, revealing fundamental inconsistency.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, which of the following is a contradiction in Jafar ibn Harb's position?
A) He divides motion by cause but not by place.
B) He divides accidents by time and place but does not divide speech by place.
C) He claims all accidents are indivisible.
D) He denies the existence of half-motions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi first undermines Jafar's credibility by exposing contradictions in his own principles before engaging the main claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, al-Nasafi exposes Jafar's inconsistency: he claims one principle but makes exceptions. Where in your own life do you hold others to a standard you don't apply to yourself? Will you ask God to show you that blind spot today?
?
Jafar treated divine speech as immune to the division he applied to other accidents. Are there areas of your faith—like your understanding of God's attributes—where you accept comfortable inconsistencies rather than seeking the full truth? What will you do to confront that discomfort?
?
The passage ends with 'God is the grantor of success.' When you wrestle with theological questions, do you truly rely on divine guidance, or do you trust your own reasoning first? What would it mean to genuinely seek success from God in understanding His nature?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Refutation of the Division of Motion by Multiple Agents
Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that one agent produces one undivided motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the unity of agent, time, and place does not guarantee the unity of an act, using the counterexample of color and taste.

Core Idea
Unity of agent, time, and place does not entail unity of the act; color and taste prove this.

Jafar ibn Harb claims that motion from a single agent is undivided because time, place, and agent are one. Al-Nasafi refutes this with a powerful counterexample: God can create a color and a taste in the same indivisible place and time, yet they remain two distinct things. This lesson examines that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read Jafar's claim and the response
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice Jafar's assertion and al-Nasafi's immediate rebuttal: 'This is ignorance.'
2
Understand the counterexample
God creates color and taste in one indivisible place at one time; they are two different accidents despite unity of agent, place, and time.
This shows that unity of external conditions does not force unity of the act.
3
Apply the counterexample to motion
Al-Nasafi applies this logic: if motion were halves for multiple agents, it would also be halves for one agent. Since it is not, the claim fails.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

زمان غير ما وجد منها في غيره من الأزمنة. ثم زعم أن الكلام الواحد يحل في أماكن كثيرة متغايرة ولم يجعله متغايرا بتغاير الأمكنة، وزعم أن أكثر الأعراض توجد في وقتين لأنه يقول ببقائها، ولم يجعل تغاير إذا حركه فكان وجد بكل واحد من المحركين حركة على حدة وحصل لكل محرك حركة على حدة، وهذا ليس بمذهبه. وإن قال: كل نصف ليس بحركة فإذا ليس كل نصف بزوال النصفان إذا اجتمعا فقد اجتمع ما ليس بزوال وما ليس بزوال Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim made by Jafar ibn Harb (known as al-Ashajj) that motion is genuinely divided into two halves when multiple agents act. The refutation proceeds in several steps. First, al-Nasafi points out the contradiction in the opponent's position: the opponent divides a single motion by reference to its cause (the agent) rather than in itself, yet he himself divides accidents by place, time, and agent. He then notes that the opponent inconsistently treats speech as one despite multiple places, and accidents as remaining over two times without dividing them. After establishing this inconsistency, al-Nasafi presents Jafar ibn Harb's specific view: the motion of each part is actually two halves, each belonging to a different impeller, and the two halves are really different. The refutation then asks: If a single agent produces motion, why would it not also be two different acts? The opponent replies that motion from one agent is one and undivided because time, place, and agent are unified. Al-Nasafi calls this ignorance and gives a counterexample: God can create a color and a taste in the same indivisible place at the same time, and they remain two different things despite unified agent, place, and time. Therefore, the unity of agent, place, and time does not necessitate unity of the act. If the opponent says yes, then each mover would produce a separate motion, which contradicts his doctrine. If he says no, then the half is not a motion, so combining two non-motions cannot cause departure; but if departure occurs, it must come from something else. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an act has one agent, one time, and one place, it must be a single, undivided entity.
Correction: The counterexample of color and taste shows that two distinct accidents can exist with identical conditions, so unity of conditions does not guarantee unity of the act.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does al-Nasafi use to refute Jafar's claim that one agent produces one undivided motion?
A) A single motion can be divided by the number of agents.
B) God creates color and taste in one place and time, yet they are two distinct things.
C) Motion and rest cannot coexist.
D) The body cannot move by half a motion.

Key Takeaway
The unity of agent, time, and place does not guarantee the unity of an act, as demonstrated by the coexistence of distinct accidents like color and taste.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform multiple actions in the same moment—like thinking, speaking, and feeling—do you tend to lump them together as one 'moment'? How does al-Nasafi's example of color and taste challenge your tendency to unify your own actions, pushing you to see the distinct realities your soul produces?
?
If unity of time, place, and agent does not guarantee unity of act, then the simultaneous mixture of good and bad deeds within you cannot be dismissed as one 'mixed' act. How will you separate them in your self-accountability before Allah, and what will you do about the bad ones right now?

The Dilemma of Half-Motion and Absurd Consequences
Al-Nasafi poses dilemmas and an absurd consequence to refute Jafar's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Jafar ibn Harb's view leads to logical dilemmas and an absurd infinite division of motion.

Core Idea
If half a motion is not a motion, then two halves cannot cause motion, and multiple agents lead to infinite division.

Al-Nasafi continues his refutation by posing two dilemmas about whether half of the alleged divided motion actually causes movement. If it does, each agent produces a separate motion; if it does not, combining two non-motions cannot produce motion. He then shows that Jafar's view leads to the absurdity that motion would be divided into tenths and sixths if many agents act.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemmas carefully
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'ثم يقال له: أيزول الجسم بنصف الحركة...' . There are two conditional questions.
Each question targets the nature of the half-motion.
2
Analyze the first dilemma
If the body departs by half the motion, then each agent produces a separate motion (contradicting Jafar's view). If not, then half is not motion, and combining non-motions cannot cause departure.
3
Analyze the second dilemma
If one agent produces half in each part, does the body depart? Yes → it is motion; No → it remains static, combining rest with some motion (impossible).
4
Consider the absurd consequence
If many agents move a body, motion would be divided into as many parts as agents, e.g., tenths, sixths, etc. This is clearly false.
Al-Nasafi uses this reduction to show the view is untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

على مسببه لا في نفسه وهذا مما لا يجد فيه فرقا البتة. ثم إنه ناقض حيث زعم أن الأعراض تنقسم باعتبار الأمكنة والأزمنة والفاعلين فيكون ما وجد منها في مكان غير ما وجد في غيره من الأمكنة، وما وجد منها في زمان غير ما وجد منها في غيره من الأزمنة. ثم زعم أن الكلام الواحد يحل في أماكن كثيرة متغايرة ولم يجعله متغايرا بتغاير الأمكنة، وزعم أن أكثر الأعراض توجد في وقتين لأنه يقول ببقائها، ولم يجعل تغاير الأزمنة موجبًا تغاير ما فيها من الأعراض، والله الموفق. وزعم جعفر بن حرب المعروف بالأشج: أن حركة كل جزء منقسمة قسمين على الحقيقة نصفها لهذا الدافع ونصفها لذلك وهي ذات بعضين متغايرين حقيقة. فقيل له: ما أنكرت أن تكون الحركة التي يفعلها واحد فعلين متغايرين أيضا. أنها فعل واحد غير منقسم لانعدام علة انقسامها لأن زمانها ومكانها وفاعلها واحد. قيل له: هذا جهل، لأن ما يتغاير في نفسه لا أثر لاتحاد الزمان والمكان والفاعل في اتحاده كما لو خلق الله تعالى في زمان واحد في جزء لا يتجزأ من الأمكنة لونًا وطعما كانا شيئين متغايرين، وإن اتحد الفاعل والمكان والزمان، فلو كانت الحركة نصفين متغايرين عند تعدد الفاعلين كانت أيضًا نصفين متغايرين عند اتحاد الفاعل، وحيث لم يكن كذلك عند اتحاد الفاعل دل أنه ليس كذلك عند تعدد الفاعلين. ثم يقال له: أيزول الجسم بنصف الحركة التي حصلها واحد منهما؟ فإن قال: نعم. فهو إذا حركه فكان وجد بكل واحد من المحركين حركة على حدة وحصل لكل محرك حركة على حدة، وهذا ليس بمذهبه. وإن قال: كل نصف ليس بحركة فإذا ليس كل نصف بزوال النصفان إذا اجتمعا فقد اجتمع ما ليس بزوال وما ليس بزوال وباجتماع ما ليس بزوال وما ليس بزوال وما ليس بحركة وما ليس بحركة لا يزول الجسم عن المكان الأول، وإذا زال دل على وجود الزوال. ثم يقال له: لو أوجد أحدهما في كل جزء من أجزاء الجسم نصف الحركة الذي هو حصته أيزول الجسم عن مكانه الأول؟ فإن قال: نعم. فهو إذا حركة على ما بينا، وإن قال: لا. فقد بقي في المكان الأول وبقي ساكنا فيه فكان فيه اجتماع السكون وبعض الحركة ويستحيل اجتماع الشيء مع بعض ما يضاده، ثم النصف الآخر أيضا ليس بحركة فيجوز أن يجتمع معه السكون أيضا إذ السكون قد يجامع ما ليس بحركة وما ليس بحركة كما يجامع اللون والطعم، وهذا كله تجاهل وخروج عن المعارف. ومقتضى هذا أن جماعة كثيرة لو حركوا جسمًا ينبغي أن تحصل حركة كل جزء متبعضة على عدد الفاعلين، فتكون كل حركة منقسمة إلى الأعشار والأسداس وأكثر، وهذا جهل فاحش.
The difference between you and those who say that the part is divisible, making what touches the earth other than what touches the sky, and what touches the north other than what touches the south, and what touches the east wind other than what touches the west wind, by analogy with your division of the single motion and the single thing upon its cause, not in itself, and this is something in which he finds no difference at all. Then he contradicts when he claims that accidents are divided with respect to places, times, and agents, so that what occurs in one place is other than what occurs in other places, and what occurs in one time is other than what occurs in other times. Then he claims that the single speech inheres in many different places and does not make it different due to difference of places, and he claims that most accidents exist in two times because he affirms their subsistence, and he does not make the difference of times necessitate the difference of the accidents in them, and God is the grantor of success. And Jafar ibn Harb, known as al-Ashajj, claimed that the motion of each part is actually divided into two halves, half belonging to this impeller and half to that, and it has two really different parts. So it was said to him: What prevents you from considering that the motion done by one agent is also two different acts? [He replied:] It is a single undivided act due to the absence of a cause for its division, because its time, place, and agent are one. It was said to him: This is ignorance, because what is different in itself, the unity of time, place, and agent has no effect on its unity, just as if God Most High created, in a single time, in an indivisible part of places, a color and a taste, they would be two different things, even though the agent, place, and time are one. So if motion were two different halves when there are multiple agents, it would also be two different halves when the agent is one, and since it is not so when the agent is one, it indicates that it is not so when there are multiple agents. Then it is said to him: Does the body depart [from its place] by the half of the motion produced by one of them? If he says: Yes. Then if he moves it, then each of the two movers would have a separate motion, and each mover would obtain a motion separately, and this is not his doctrine. If he says: Every half is not a motion, then not every half is a departure; when the two halves combine, then that which is not a departure and that which is not a departure have combined, and by the combination of what is not a departure and what is not a departure and what is not a motion (and what is not a motion does not cause the body to depart) the body does not depart from its first place, and if it does depart, it indicates the existence of departure. Then it is said to him: If one of them produced in each part of the parts of the body the half of the motion that is his share, does the body depart from its first place? If he says: Yes. Then it is a motion as we explained. If he says: No. Then it remains in the first place and remains static in it, so there would be the combination of rest and some motion, and it is impossible for a thing to combine with some of its opposite. Then the other half is also not a motion, so it is possible for rest to combine with it as well, since rest may accompany what is not a motion and what is not a motion, just as it accompanies color and taste. All this is feigned ignorance and departure from knowledge. And the consequence of this is that if a large group moved a body, the motion of each part would become fragmented according to the number of agents, so that each motion would be divided into tenths, sixths, and more, and this is egregious ignorance.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim made by Jafar ibn Harb (known as al-Ashajj) that motion is genuinely divided into two halves when multiple agents act. The refutation proceeds in several steps. First, al-Nasafi points out the contradiction in the opponent's position: the opponent divides a single motion by reference to its cause (the agent) rather than in itself, yet he himself divides accidents by place, time, and agent. He then notes that the opponent inconsistently treats speech as one despite multiple places, and accidents as remaining over two times without dividing them. After establishing this inconsistency, al-Nasafi presents Jafar ibn Harb's specific view: the motion of each part is actually two halves, each belonging to a different impeller, and the two halves are really different. The refutation then asks: If a single agent produces motion, why would it not also be two different acts? The opponent replies that motion from one agent is one and undivided because time, place, and agent are unified. Al-Nasafi calls this ignorance and gives a counterexample: God can create a color and a taste in the same indivisible place at the same time, and they remain two different things despite unified agent, place, and time. Therefore, the unity of agent, place, and time does not necessitate unity of the act. So if motion were two halves when agents are multiple, it would also be two halves when the agent is one—but since it is not so for one agent, it is not so for multiple agents. Next, al-Nasafi poses a dilemma about whether the body departs from its place by half of the motion. If the opponent says yes, then each mover would produce a separate motion, which contradicts his doctrine. If he says no, then the half is not a motion, so combining two non-motions cannot cause departure; but if departure occurs, it must come from something else. Then another dilemma: if one mover produces half the motion in each part, does the body depart? If yes, then it is a motion (which contradicts the claim that it is only half). If no, then the body remains static, combining rest with some motion—impossible because motion and rest are opposites. Moreover, the other half is also not a motion, so rest can coexist with it, just as rest coexists with color and taste. Al-Nasafi concludes that this entire position is feigned ignorance and a departure from true knowledge. He further shows that the view leads to absurdity: if many agents move a body, the motion would be divided into as many parts as agents, leading to infinite division (tenths, sixths, etc.), which is clearly false.

Key Terms
حركة (motion)
سكون (rest)
جزء لا يتجزأ (indivisible part)
فاعل (agent)
زوال (departure/removal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Half a motion can still be considered a motion and can cause movement.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that if half is not a motion, then two halves cannot combine to produce motion; if it is a motion, then each agent produces a separate motion, contradicting Jafar's view.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is an absurd consequence of Jafar ibn Harb's view if many agents move a body?
A) The body would not move at all.
B) Motion would be divided into tenths, sixths, etc.
C) The agents would cancel each other's motion.
D) The body would move faster.

Key Takeaway
Jafar ibn Harb's view of divided motion leads to logical contradictions and the absurd conclusion that motion can be infinitely fragmented, making it untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage concludes that Jafar's view is 'egregious ignorance.' When you hold a belief that contradicts clear logical reasoning, do you persist out of pride, or are you willing to submit to the truth even when it feels uncomfortable? What would it take for you to admit that you were wrong about a deeply held conviction?
?
Al-Nasafi exposes a contradiction: if half-motion is not motion, then combining two non-motions cannot produce motion. Are there areas in your life where you are trying to combine ‘half-truths’ or partial commitments, expecting them to produce the full reality of faith? Will you examine each part with honesty?
?
The text calls this 'feigned ignorance and departure from knowledge.' Is there any area of your understanding of Allah's attributes or the nature of His actions where you are deliberately ignoring an inconsistency because acknowledging it would demand a change in how you think or live? Are you willing to pursue knowledge at the cost of your comfort?

Refutation of Ibn al-Rawandi on Motion and Agent
Nasafi reports and refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's view identifying tahrīk and haraka.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying the act of causing motion (tahrīk) with motion (haraka) itself leads to a logical contradiction, and how this refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's position.

Core Idea
Causing motion cannot be identical to motion, or else every cause would produce multiple effects.

Nasafi reports a peculiar view attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi, who claimed that the act of causing motion (tahrīk) is the same as motion (haraka). At first glance, this might seem like a minor semantic point, but Nasafi shows that it leads to a devastating logical contradiction. By unpacking the argument, we see why such a conflation is untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: التحريك (al-tahrīk) and الحركة (al-ḥaraka). Note how Nasafi first reports Ibn al-Rawandi's view, then draws out its consequences, then dismisses it.
The passage is dense; take time to follow the logical flow.
2
Identify the core claim and its consequence
Ibn al-Rawandi claims that tahrīk is identical to haraka. Nasafi shows that if two agents are both causing motion, then from each agent a separate motion must arise. This contradicts the idea that a single agent produces a single motion.
3
Understand the refutation
Nasafi points out that this consequence contradicts Ibn al-Rawandi's own doctrine, and then dismisses the whole argument as nonsense. He notes that the falsity of atomism (tajazzu' al-a'rād) is already known by natural disposition.
This shows the importance of distinguishing cause from effect in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكى ابن الراوندي عنه أنه كان يقول: التحريك هو عين الحركة، فلما كان التحريك والحركة واحدًا والدافعان محركان فالواحد يكون محركا، وإنما يكون محركًا أن لو وجد منه تحريك، والتحريك هو عين الحركة فيجب أن يحصل بكل واحد منهما حركة على حدة وهذا خلاف مذهبه وهذا كله هذيان، وتضييع الوقت بإبطاله عبث، إذ معرفة بطلان القول بتجزؤ الأعراض حاصلة بالطبائع، والله الموفق.
Ibn al-Rawandi related from him that he used to say: The causing of motion (al-tahrīk) is the very essence of motion (al-ḥaraka). Since causing motion and motion are one, and the two pushers are both causing motion, then a single thing would be causing motion. For it to be causing motion, only if causing motion proceeds from it, and causing motion is the very essence of motion; so it follows that from each one of them a motion occurs separately. This contradicts his doctrine. All of this is nonsense, and wasting time in refuting it is futile, for the knowledge of the falsity of the doctrine of the atomization of accidents is attained by natural disposition. Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage reports and refutes a position attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi, who claimed that the act of causing motion (al-tahrīk) is identical to motion (al-ḥaraka) itself. The refutation points out the logical consequence: if two agents are both causing motion, and causing motion is the same as motion, then from each agent a separate motion must arise. This would contradict the idea that a single agent produces a single motion. The author dismisses the argument as nonsense and notes that the falsity of atomism (tajazzu' al-a'rād) is already clear by natural disposition. He concludes by invoking divine aid.

Key Terms
التحريك
الحركة
تجزؤ الأعراض
ابن الراوندي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That identifying the cause (tahrīk) with the effect (haraka) is a coherent position.
Correction: The text shows that if they are identical, then from two agents causing motion, two separate motions would arise, contradicting the principle that a single action produces a single effect. This reveals the incoherence.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn al-Rawandi's reported view claim about tahrīk and haraka?
A) They are distinct.
B) They are identical.
C) They are opposites.
D) One causes the other.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Rawandi's identification of causing motion with motion itself is incoherent because it leads to multiple effects from a single cause, contradicting basic principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you heard a claim that seemed obviously false, like Ibn al-Rawandi's identification of cause and effect, would you waste time arguing it or trust your natural disposition? What does your reaction reveal about your patience and reliance on Allah's guidance?
?
Do you hold any belief simply because it feels 'natural' without examining its coherence? How would you respond if someone showed it leads to a contradiction? Would you admit error or defend it out of pride?
?
Nasafi says refuting such nonsense is a 'waste of time'. Is there an area in your life where you engage in pointless debates instead of focusing on what is truly beneficial for your deen? What could you replace that time with?
